Environment","Name":"Science &amp; Environment","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Science &amp; Environment"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:51:45.984509Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"1aa4ccec-87b5-44b8-b5af-c553debaf6f6","Id":"wwfuture/section/science-environment","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:51:20.083392Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/science-environment"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/science-environment","_id":"559a66a082f2d2dbf7bcb96e"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Health","Name":"Health","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Health"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:44:06.503434Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"00a9a33a-e393-4d09-8029-7e9d5bd1d8cb","Id":"wwfuture/section/health","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:52:37.074442Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/health"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/health","_id":"559a66a082f2d2dbf7bcb96d"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-04-01T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Jane Goodall: How she redefined mankind","HeadlineShort":"The woman who redefined mankind","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Primatologist Jane Goodall, turning 80 this week, tells Henry Nicholls the inside story on how she transformed our understanding of chimpanzees, what it means to be human, and the controversy and condescension she faced in her influential career.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;The meandering Moei river marks the natural boundary between Thailand and Myanmar. Its muddy waters are at their fullest, but Fran&ccedil;ois Nosten still crosses them in just a minute, aboard a narrow, wooden boat. In the dry season, he could wade across. As he steps onto the western riverbank, in Myanmar, he passes no checkpoint and presents no passport.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The air is cool. After months of rain, the surrounding jungle pops with vivid lime and emerald hues. Nosten climbs a set of wooden slats that wind away from the bank, up a muddy slope. His pace, as ever, seems relaxed and out of kilter with his almost permanently grave expression and urgent purpose. Nosten, a rangy Frenchman with tousled brown hair and glasses, is one of the world&rsquo;s leading experts on malaria. He is here to avert a looming disaster. At the top of the slope, he reaches a small village of simple wooden buildings with tin and thatch roofs. This is Hka Naw Tah, home to around 400 people and a testing ground for Nosten&rsquo;s bold plan to completely stamp out malaria from this critical corner of the world.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Malaria is the work of the single-celled &lt;em&gt;Plasmodium&lt;/em&gt; parasites, with &lt;em&gt;Plasmodium falciparum&lt;/em&gt; being chief among them. They spread between people through the bites of mosquitoes, invading first the liver, then the red blood cells. The first symptoms are generic and flu-like: fever, headache, sweats and chills, vomiting. At that point, the immune system usually curtails the infection. But if the parasites spread to the kidneys, lungs and brain, things go downhill quickly. Organs start failing. Infected red blood cells clog the brain&rsquo;s blood vessels, depriving it of oxygen and leading to seizures, unconsciousness and death.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w62tp\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When Nosten first arrived in south-east Asia almost 30 years ago, malaria was the biggest killer in the region. Artemisinin changed everything. Spectacularly fast and effective, the drug arrived on the scene in 1994, when options for treating malaria were running out. Since then, &ldquo;cases have just gone down, down, down,&rdquo; says Nosten. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve never seen so few in the rainy season &ndash; a few hundred this year compared to tens of thousands before.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But he has no time for celebration. Artemisinin used to clear &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;in a day; now it can take several. The parasite has started to become resistant. The wonder drug is failing. It is the latest reprise of a decades-long theme: we attack malaria with a new drug, it mounts an evolutionary riposte.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Back in his office, Nosten pulls up a map showing the current whereabouts of the resistant parasites. Three coloured bands highlight the borders between Cambodia and Vietnam, Cambodia and Thailand, and Thailand and Myanmar (Burma). Borders. Bold lines on maps, but invisible in reality. A river that can be crossed in a rickety boat is no barrier to a parasite that rides in the salivary glands of mosquitoes or the red blood cells of humans.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;History tells us what happens next. Over the last century, almost every frontline antimalarial drug &ndash; chloroquine, sulfadoxine, pyrimethamine &ndash; has become obsolete because of defiant parasites that emerged from western Cambodia. From this cradle of resistance, the parasites gradually spread west to Africa, causing the deaths of millions. Malaria already kills around 660,000 people every year, and most of them are African kids. If artemisinin resistance reached that continent, it would be catastrophic, especially since there are no good replacement drugs on the immediate horizon.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w632f\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Nosten thinks that without radical measures, resistance will spread to India and Bangladesh. Once that happens, it will be too late. Those countries are too big, too populous, too uneven in their health services to even dream about containing the resistant parasites. Once there, they will inevitably spread further. He thinks it will happen in three years, maybe four. &ldquo;Look at the speed of change on this border. It&rsquo;s exponential. It&rsquo;s not going to take 10 or 15 years to reach Bangladesh. It&rsquo;ll take just a few. We have to do something before it&rsquo;s too late.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hundreds of scientists are developing innovative new ways of dealing with malaria, from potential vaccines to new drugs, genetically modified mosquitoes to lethal fungi. As Nosten sees it, none of these will be ready in time. The only way of stopping artemisinin resistance, he says, is to completely remove malaria from its cradle of resistance. &ldquo;If you want to eliminate artemisinin resistance, you have to eliminate malaria,&rdquo; says Nosten. Not control it, not contain it. Eliminate it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That makes the Moei river more than a border between nations. It&rsquo;s Stalingrad. It&rsquo;s Thermopylae. It&rsquo;s the last chance for halting the creeping obsolescence of our best remaining drug. What happens here will decide the fate of millions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Weapon failure&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;The world tried to eliminate malaria 60 years ago. Malaria was a global affliction back then, infecting hundreds of thousands of troops during World War II. This helped motivate a swell of postwar research. To fight the disease, in 1946 the United States created what is now the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC), the country&rsquo;s premier public health institute. After a decisive national eradication programme, the nation became malaria-free in 1951. Brazil had also controlled a burgeoning malaria epidemic with insecticides.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Meanwhile, new weapons had emerged. The long-lasting insecticide DDT was already being widely used and killed mosquitoes easily. A new drug called chloroquine did the same to Plasmodium. Armed with these tools and buoyed by earlier successes, the World Health Organization formally launched the Global Malaria Eradication Programme in 1955. DDT was sprayed in countless homes. Chloroquine was even added to table salt in some countries. It was as ambitious a public health initiative as has ever been attempted.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w63qc\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It worked to a point. Malaria fell dramatically in Taiwan, Sri Lanka, India, the Caribbean, the Balkans, and parts of the south Pacific. But ultimately the problem was too big, the plan too ambitious. It barely made a dent in sub-Saharan Africa, where public health infrastructure was poor and malaria was most prevalent. And its twin pillars soon crumbled as &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;evolved resistance to chloroquine and mosquitoes evolved resistance to DDT. The disease bounced back across much of Asia and the western Pacific.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 1969, the eradication programme was finally abandoned. Despite several successes, its overall failure had a chilling impact on malaria research. Investments from richer (and now unaffected) countries dwindled, save for a spike of interest during the Vietnam War. The best minds in the field left for fresher challenges. Malaria, now a tropical disease of poor people, became unfashionable.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&ldquo;Rosbif and the frog&rdquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Fran&ccedil;ois Nosten always wanted to travel. His father, a sailor on merchant ships, returned home with stories of far-flung adventures and instilled a deep wanderlust. Nosten&rsquo;s original plan was to work on overseas development projects, but one of his teachers pushed him down a different path. &ldquo;He said the best thing you can do if you want to travel anywhere is to be a doctor. That&rsquo;s why I started medical school.&rdquo; As soon as he graduated, he joined M&eacute;decins Sans Fronti&egrave;res and started living the dream. He flew off to Africa and South-east Asia, before arriving in Thailand in 1983. There, he started treating refugees from Myanmar in camps along the Thai border.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 1985, an English visitor arrived at the camps and Nosten took him for a random tourist until he started asking insightful questions about malaria. That man was Nick White. A British clinician, he was drawn to Bangkok in 1980 by the allure of the tropics and a perverse desire to study something unfashionable. The University of Oxford had just set up a new tropical medicine research unit in collaboration with Bangkok&rsquo;s Mahidol University, and White was the third to join.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The rosbif and the frog&rdquo;, as Nosten puts it, bonded over an interest in malaria, a desire to knuckle down and get things done, and a similar grouchy conviviality. They formed a close friendship and started working together.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w6cjn\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 1986, they set up a field station for White&rsquo;s Bangkok research unit: little more than a centrifuge and microscope within Nosten&rsquo;s rickety house. Three years later, Nosten moved to Shoklo, the largest refugee camp along the Thai&ndash;Myanmar border and home to around 9,000 people. Most were Karen &ndash; the third largest of Myanmar&rsquo;s 130 or so ethnic groups &ndash; who were fleeing persecution from the majority Bamar government. Nosten worked out of a bamboo hospital &ndash; the first Shoklo Malaria Research Unit.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Malaria was rife. Floods were regular. Military leaders from both Thailand and Myanmar occasionally ordered Nosten to leave. Without any electricity, he often had to use a mirror to angle sunlight into his microscope. He loved it. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not a city person,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;I couldn&rsquo;t survive in Bangkok very well. I wasn&rsquo;t alone in Shoklo but it was sufficiently remote.&rdquo; The immediacy of the job and the lack of bureaucracy also appealed. He could try out new treatments and see their impact right away. He trained local people to detect &lt;em&gt;Plasmodium&lt;/em&gt; under a microscope and help with research. He even met his future wife &ndash; a Karen teacher named Colley Paw, who is now one of his right-hand researchers (White was the best man at their wedding). These were the best years of his life.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Shoklo years ended in 1995 after a splinter faction of Karen started regularly attacking the camps, in a bid to force the refugees back into Myanmar. &ldquo;They came in and started shooting,&rdquo; says Nosten. &ldquo;We once had to hide in a hole for the night, with bullets flying around.&rdquo; The Thai military, unable to defend the scattered camps, consolidated them into a single site called Mae La &ndash; a dense lattice of thatch-roofed houses built on stilts, which now contains almost 50,000 people. Nosten went with them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w6chb\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He has since expanded the Shoklo Unit into a huge hand that stretches across the region. Its palm is a central laboratory in the town of Mae Sot, where Nosten lives, and the fingers are clinics situated in border settlements, each with trained personnel and sophisticated facilities. The one in Mae La has a $250,000 neonatal care machine, and can cope with everything short of major surgery. Nosten has also set up small &lsquo;malaria posts&rsquo; along the border. These are typically just volunteer farmers with a box of diagnostic tests and medicine in their house.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know anybody else who could have done what Fran&ccedil;ois has done,&rdquo; says White. &ldquo;He&rsquo;ll underplay the difficulties but between the physical dangers, politics, logistical nightmares, and the fraught conditions of the refugees, it&rsquo;s not been easy. He&rsquo;s not a shrinking violet.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thanks to Nosten&rsquo;s network, locals know where to go if they feel unwell, and they are never far from treatments. That is vital. If infected people are treated within 48 hours of their first symptoms, their parasites die before they get a chance to enter another mosquito and the cycle of malaria breaks. &ldquo;You deploy early identification and treatment, and malaria goes away,&rdquo; says Nosten. &ldquo;Everywhere we&rsquo;ve done this, it&rsquo;s worked.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;New hope?&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; When Nosten and White teamed up in the 1980s, their first success was showing that a new drug called mefloquine was excellent at curing malaria, and at preventing it in pregnant women. Most drugs had fallen to resistant parasites and the last effective one &ndash; quinine &ndash; involved a week of nasty side-effects. Mefloquine was a godsend.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But within five years, &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;had started to resist it too. &ldquo;We tried different things like increasing the dose, but we were clearly losing the drug,&rdquo; says Nosten. &ldquo;We saw more and more treatment failures, patients coming back weeks later with the same malaria. We were really worried that we wouldn&rsquo;t have any more options.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Salvation came from China. In 1967, Chairman Mao Zedong launched a covert military initiative to discover new antimalarial drugs, partly to help his North Vietnamese allies, who were losing troops to the disease. It was called Project 523. A team of some 600 scientists scoured 200 herbs used in traditional Chinese medicine for possible antimalarial chemicals. They found a clear winner in 1971 &ndash; a common herb called qing hao (Artemisia annua or sweet wormwood). Using hints from a 2,000-year-old recipe for treating haemorrhoids, they isolated the herb&rsquo;s active ingredient, characterised it, tested it in humans and animals, and created synthetic versions. &ldquo;This was in the aftermath of the Cultural Revolution,&rdquo; says White. &ldquo;Society had been ripped apart, there was still a lot of oppression, and facilities were poor. But they did some extremely good chemistry.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The results were miraculous. The new drug annihilated even severe forms of chloroquine-resistant malaria, and did so with unparalleled speed and no side-effects. The team named it Qinghaosu. The West would know it as artemisinin. Or, at least, they would when they found out about it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w6795\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Project 523 was shrouded in secrecy, and few results were published. Qinghaosu was already being widely used in China and Vietnam when the first English description appeared in the Chinese Medical Journal in 1979. Western scientists, suspicious about Chinese journals and traditional medicine, greeted it with scepticism and wasted time trying to develop their own less effective versions. The Chinese, meanwhile, were reluctant to share their new drug with Cold War enemies.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;During this political stalemate, White saw a tattered copy of the 1979 paper. He travelled to China in 1981, and returned with a vial of the drug, which he still keeps in a drawer in his office. He and Nosten began studying it, working out the right doses, and testing the various derivatives.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;They realised that artemisinin&rsquo;s only shortcoming was a lack of stamina. People clear it so quickly from their bodies that they need seven daily doses to completely cure themselves. Few complete the full course. White&rsquo;s ingenious solution was to pair the new drug with mefloquine &ndash; a slower-acting but longer-lasting partner. Artemisinin would land a brutal shock-and-awe strike that destroyed the majority of parasites, mefloquine would mop up the survivors. If any parasites resisted the artemisinin assault, mefloquine would finish them off. &lt;em&gt;Plasmodium&lt;/em&gt; would need to resist both drugs to survive the double whammy, and White deemed that unlikely. Just three days of this artemisinin combination therapy (ACT) was enough to treat virtually every case of malaria. In theory, ACTs should have been resistance-proof.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Nosten started using them along the Thai&ndash;Myanmar border in 1994 and immediately saw results. Quinine took days to clear the parasites and left people bed-ridden for a week with dizzy spells. ACTs had them returning to work after 24 hours.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w689n\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But victories in malaria are often short-lived. In the early 2000s, the team started hearing rumours from western Cambodia that ACTs were becoming less effective. White tried to stay calm. He had heard plenty of false alarms about incurable Cambodian patients, but it always turned out that they were taking counterfeit drugs. &ldquo;I was just hoping it was another of those,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It was not. In 2006, Harald Noedl from the Medical University of Vienna started checking out the rumours for himself. In the Cambodian village of Ta Sanh, he treated 60 malaria patients with artesunate (an artemisinin derivative) and found that two of them carried exceptionally stubborn parasites. These infections cleared in four to six days, rather than the usual two. And even though the patients stayed in a clinic outside any malaria hotspots, their parasites returned a few weeks later.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I first presented those data in November 2007 and as expected, people were very sceptical,&rdquo; says Noedl. After all, a pair of patients is an epidemiological blip. Still, this was worrying enough to prompt White&rsquo;s team to run their own study in another nearby village. They got even worse news. The 40 people they treated with artesunate took an average of 3.5 days to clear their parasites, and six of them suffered from rebounding infections within a month. &ldquo;Rapid parasite clearance is the hallmark of artemisinins,&rdquo; says Arjen Dondorp, one of White&rsquo;s colleagues based in Bangkok. &ldquo;That property suddenly disappeared.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Despite the hopes that ACTs would forestall artemisinin&rsquo;s expiry, resistance had arrived, just as it had done for other antimalarials. And, as if to rub salt in the wound, it had come from the same damn place.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Hyper mutators&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; Why has a small corner of western Cambodia, no bigger than Wales or New Jersey, repeatedly given rise to drug-beating parasites?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;White thinks that the most likely explanation is the region&rsquo;s unregulated use of antimalarial drugs. China supplied artemisinin to the tyrannical Khmer Rouge in the late 1970s, giving Cambodians access to it almost two decades before White conceived of ACTs. Few used it correctly. Some got ineffective doses from counterfeit pills. Others took a couple of tablets and stopped once their fever disappeared. &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;was regularly exposed to artemisinin without being completely wiped out, and the most resistant parasites survived to spread to new hosts. There is a saying among malariologists: &ldquo;The last man standing is the most resistant.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Genetic studies hint at other explanations. Early last year, Dominic Kwiatkowski from the University of Oxford showed that some &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;strains from west Cambodia have mutations in genes that repair faults in their DNA, much like some cancer cells or antibiotic-resistant bacteria. In other words, they have mutations that make them prone to mutating. This might also explain why, in lab experiments, they develop drug resistance more quickly than strains from other parts of the world. Evolution is malaria&rsquo;s greatest weapon, and these &lsquo;hypermutators&rsquo; evolve in fifth gear.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Kwiatkowski&rsquo;s team also found that &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;is spookily diverse in west Cambodia. It is home to three artemisinin-resistant populations that are genetically distinct, despite living in the same small area. That is bizarre. Without obvious barriers between them, the strains ought to regularly mate and share their genes. Instead, they seem to shun each other&rsquo;s company. They are so inbred that they consist almost entirely of clones.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w6984\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Kwiatkowski suspects that these parasites descended from some lucky genetic lottery winners that accumulated the right sets of mutations for evading artemisinin. When they mate with other strains, their winning tickets break up and their offspring are wiped out by the drug. Only their inbred progeny, which keep the right combinations, survive and spread.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It undoubtedly helps that south-east Asia does not have much malaria. In West Africa, where transmission is high, a child might be infected with three to five &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;strains at any time, giving them many opportunities to mate and shuffle their genes. A Cambodian child, however, usually sees one strain at a time, and is a poor hook-up spot for the parasite. The region&rsquo;s infrastructure may also have helped to enforce the parasites&rsquo; isolation: local roads are poor, and people&rsquo;s movements were long constrained by the Khmer Rouge.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;West Cambodia, then, could be rife with &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;strains that are especially prone to evolving resistance, that get many opportunities to do so because antimalarial drugs are abused, and that easily hold on to their drug-beating mutations once they get them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;These are plausible ideas, but hard to verify since we still know very little about how exactly the parasites resist a drug. Earlier cases of resistance were largely due to mutations in single genes &ndash; trump cards that immediately made for invincible parasites. A small tweak in the crt gene, and &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;can suddenly pump chloroquine out of its cells. A few tweaks to dhps and dhfr, the genes targeted by sulfadoxine and pyrimethamine, and the drug can no longer stick to its targets.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w67qd\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Artemisinin seems to be a trickier enemy. Curiously, &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;takes a long time to evolve resistance to artemisinin in lab experiments, much longer than in the wild. Those strains that do tend to be weak and unstable. &ldquo;I suspect you need a complicated series of genetic changes to make a parasite that&rsquo;s not lethally unfit in the presence of these drugs,&rdquo; says White. &ldquo;It would be unusual if this was a single mutation.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Practices such as unregulated drug use and misuse may help encourage and accelerate the rate of such changes out in the field. Kwiatkowski&rsquo;s study suggests that the parasites may have evolved artemisinin resistance several times over, perhaps through a different route each time. Several groups are racing to find the responsible mutations, with news of the first few breaking in December 2013. That&rsquo;s the key to quickly identifying resistant parasites and treating patients more efficiently. (Currently, you can only tell if someone has artemisinin-resistant malaria by treating them and seeing how long they take to get better.) &ldquo;We want to be able to track resistance using blood spots on filter paper,&rdquo; says Chris Plowe at the University of Maryland School of Medicine, whose group is one of those in the race.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But time is running out. From its origins in Cambodia, resistance has reached the Thai&ndash;Myanmar border. Nosten has shown that the proportion of patients who are still infected after three days of ACT has increased from zero in 2000 to 28% in 2011. Most are still being cured, but as artemisinin becomes less effective, its partner drug will have to mop up more surviving parasites. Plasmodium will evolve resistance to the partner more quickly, driving both drugs towards uselessness.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This is already happening in western Cambodia, where ACTs are failing up to a quarter of the time and many people are still infected a month later. Long-lasting infections will provide parasites with more chances to jump into mosquitoes, and then into healthy humans. Malaria cases will rise. Deaths will follow. &ldquo;This is the silence before the storm,&rdquo; says Arjen Dondorp. &ldquo;The threat is still slightly abstract and there&rsquo;s still not that much malaria, which doesn&rsquo;t help with a sense of urgency. If we suddenly see malaria exploding, then it&rsquo;ll be a clear emergency, but it&rsquo;ll also be too late.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Radical change&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; In his office at Mahidol University, Nick White is surrounded by yellowing monographs of old malaria research and overlooked by a wall-mounted mosaic of drug packets made by his daughter. He is now the chairman of the Mahidol&ndash;Oxford Tropical Medicine Research Unit and a mentor to the dozens of researchers within. He is gently ranting.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Everything to do with change in malaria meets with huge resistance,&rdquo; he says. He means political resistance, not the drug kind. He means the decade it took for the international community to endorse ACTs despite the evidence that they worked. He means the &ldquo;treacle of bureaucracy&rdquo; that he and Nosten swim through in their push to eliminate malaria.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The global response to artemisinin resistance has been a bit pathetic. Everyone will tell you how important it is and there have been any number of bloody meetings. But there is little appetite for radical change.&rdquo; He misses the old days when &ldquo;you could drive a Land Rover across borders in your khaki shorts and spray things and do stuff&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;From the outside, things look rosier. Malaria is fashionable again, and international funding has gone up by 15 times in the last decade. Big organisations seem to be rallying behind the banner of elimination. In April, the World Health Organisation published a strategy called The Emergency Response to Artemisinin Resistance.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;It&rsquo;s a marvellous plan,&rdquo; White says drily. &ldquo;It says all the right things, but we haven&rsquo;t done anything.&rdquo; It follows two other strategies that were published in 2011 and 2012, neither of which slowed the spread of artemisinin resistance. Elimination became a dirty word after the noisy failures of the 1950s and 60s, and the new strategies look like the same old tactics for controlling malaria, presented under the guise of eradicating it. &ldquo;They&rsquo;re prescriptions for inertia,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w6bs4\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Worse, they are channelling funds into ineffective measures. Take insecticide-treated bednets, a mainstay of malaria control. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ve had meetings with WHO consultants who said, &lsquo;We don&rsquo;t want to hear a word against bednets. They always work.&rsquo; But how cost-effective are they, and what&rsquo;s the evidence they work in this region? The mosquitoes here bite early in the evening. And who&rsquo;s getting malaria? Young men. Are they all tucked up in their bednets by 6 o&rsquo;clock? No. They&rsquo;re in the fields and forests. Come on! It&rsquo;s obvious.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He says that resources could be better devoted to getting rid of fake drugs and monotherapies where artemisinin is not paired with a partner. That would preserve ACTs for as long as possible. The world also needs better surveillance for resistant parasites. White is helping with that by chairing the World-Wide Anti-Malarial Resistance Network &ndash; a global community of scientists who are rapidly collecting data on how quickly patients respond to drugs, the presence of resistance genes, the numbers of fake drugs, and more.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;White also wants to know if artemisinin-resistant parasites from south-east Asia can spread in African mosquitoes. Hundreds of mosquito species can transmit malaria, but &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;is picky about its hosts. If resistant strains need time to adapt to new carriers, they might be slow to spread westwards. If they can immediately jump into far-off species, they are a plane ride away from Africa. &ldquo;That changes your containment strategy,&rdquo; says White, &ldquo;but stupidly, it&rsquo;s cut out of every research application we&rsquo;ve ever made.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He is pessimistic. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m pretty confident we won&rsquo;t win but I think we should try a lot harder than we have been. If we didn&rsquo;t pull out all the stops and kids start dying of artemisinin-resistant malaria, and we can trace the genetic origins of those parasites to south-east Asia, we shouldn&rsquo;t sleep easy in our beds.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Elimination plan&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; When Nosten&rsquo;s team first arrived at Hka Naw Tah in February, they slept and worked from the village&rsquo;s unassuming temple. Using development funds from their grant, they put up a water tower and supplied electricity for the local school. In return, the villagers built them a clinic &ndash; a spacious, open-sided hut with a sloping tin roof, benches sitting on a dirt floor, a couple of tables holding boxes of drugs and diagnostic kits, treatment rooms, and a computer station. It took just two days to erect.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Karen respect strong leadership but there is an easy-going camaraderie in the clinic. When we arrive, one of the research assistants is napping across a bench. Nosten walks over and sits on him. &ldquo;You see, and I think this is a good sign, that it&rsquo;s hard to tell who&rsquo;s the boss and who&rsquo;s the patient,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Most of the villagers don&rsquo;t seem sick, but many of them have malaria nonetheless. Until recently, Nosten&rsquo;s team had always searched for the parasites by examining a drop of blood under a microscope. If someone is sick, you can see and count the Plasmodium in their red blood cells. But in 2010, they started collecting millilitres of blood &ndash; a thousand times more than the usual drops &ndash; and searching for Plasmodium&rsquo;s DNA. Suddenly, the proportion of infected people shot up from 10&ndash;20% to 60&ndash;80%. There are three, four, maybe six times as many infected people as he thought.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w6c33\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;We didn&rsquo;t believe it at first,&rdquo; says Nosten, &ldquo;but we confirmed it and re-confirmed it.&rdquo; Perhaps the tests were giving false positives, or picking up floating DNA from dead parasites? No such luck &ndash; when the team treated people with ACTs, the hidden parasites disappeared. They were real.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;These &lsquo;sub-microscopic infections&rsquo; completely change the game for elimination. Treating the sick is no longer good enough because the disease could bounce back from the hordes of symptomless carriers. The strike will have to be swift and decisive. If it&rsquo;s half-hearted, the most resistant parasites will survive and start afresh. In malarial zones, you need to treat almost everyone, clearing the parasites they didn&rsquo;t even know they had. This is Nosten&rsquo;s goal in the border villages like Hka Naw Tah. He has support from the Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation, one of the few large funders to have truly grasped the urgency of the situation and who are &ldquo;very much in the mood for elimination&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Killing the parasites is easy: it just involves three days of ACTs. Getting healthy people to turn up to a clinic and take their medicine is much harder. The team have spent months on engagement and education. The clinic is dotted with posters explaining the symptoms of malaria and the biology of mosquitoes. Earlier this morning, Honey Moon, a Karen woman who is one of Nosten&rsquo;s oldest colleagues, knocked on the doors of all the absentees from the last round to persuade them to come for tests. As a result, 16 newcomers turned up for treatments, bringing the team closer to the full 393. Nosten is pleased. &ldquo;In this village, I&rsquo;m quite optimistic that most people will be free of the parasite,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another village down the river is proving more difficult. They are more socially conservative and have a poorer understanding of healthcare. There are two factions of Karen there, one of which is refusing to take part to spite their rivals. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a good lesson for us,&rdquo; says Nosten. &ldquo;These situations will be elsewhere.&rdquo; Eliminating malaria is not just about having the right drug, the deadliest insecticide, or the most sensitive diagnostic test. It is about knowing people, from funders to villagers. &ldquo;The most important component is getting people to agree and participate,&rdquo; says Nosten. It matters that he has been working in the region for 30 years, that the Shoklo unit is a familiar and trusted name in these parts, that virtually all his team are Karen. These are the reasons that give Nosten hope, despite the lack of political will.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If the strategy looks like it is working after a year, they will start scaling up. Eventually, they hope to cover the entire sinuous border. I ask Nosten if he would ever consider leaving. He pauses. &ldquo;Even if I wanted to go somewhere else, I&rsquo;m more or less a prisoner of my own making,&rdquo; he says. He would need to find a replacement first &ndash; a leader who would command respect among both the Karen and malaria researchers, and would be willing to relocate to a place as remote as Mae Sot. It is hard to imagine a second person who would tick all those boxes. Surrounded by airborne parasites, spreading resistance, and border-hopping refugees, Fran&ccedil;ois Nosten is stuck. He would not have it any other way.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;This article was fist appeared in &lt;a href=\"http://www.mosaicscience.com/\"&gt;Mosaic&lt;/a&gt;, which &lt;/em&gt;&lt;em&gt;is dedicated to exploring the science of life,&lt;em&gt; and reproduced under a Creative Commons licence. For more Mosaic&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/em&gt; &lt;em&gt;articles &lt;a href=\"http://www.mosaicscience.com\"&gt;click here&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-03-25T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"How malaria defeats our drugs","HeadlineShort":"Malaria's next ticking timebomb","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Our best drug hope for tackling the global killer is becoming resistant in a small region in south-east Asia. What happens next will decide the fate of millions.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":[],"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Our best drug hope for tackling the global killer is becoming resistant in a tiny region in south-east Asia. What happens next will decide the fate of millions.","SummaryShort":"Wonder drug failing in south-east Asia","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:56Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"d3b5c377-23e0-4556-b5c3-48a4a278775a","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140325-malarias-next-ticking-timebomb","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:56Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140325-malarias-next-ticking-timebomb"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140325-malarias-next-ticking-timebomb","_id":"559a6a4482f2d2dbf7bd4df1"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Is adopting a child a benevolent or a foolish act? If you were taking a cold evolutionary perspective, it would appear to be the latter.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For foster parents, there are huge costs involved, with no promise of passing on genes. Scientists have long been interested in adoption, because it seems to be wholly altruistic. But this makes it especially perplexing in animals, who do not have the cultural influences we do. So could taking a closer look at adoption in non-humans shed any light on why it&rsquo;s so common?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of the more striking places to see adoption in the animal kingdom is Ano Nuevo Island, rising from the sea less than one kilometre off the rocky California coast. Once a year, it is host to the breeding of hundreds of northern elephant seals.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;From 1976 onwards, marine scientist Marianne Riedman, together with her colleague Burney Le Boeuf, studied adoption among the seals &ndash; and why it was happening. It's a crowded beach, with bad weather, high tides and rough surf, which perhaps explains why one-quarter to two-thirds of pups each year were separated from their mothers at least once &ndash; some permanently.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01tqlkp\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The researchers counted a total of 572 orphaned pups over the course of the four consecutive breeding seasons. Riedman charmingly &lt;a href=\"http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/BF00300063\"&gt;referred to the ones that were adopted as \"alien young\"&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Intriguingly, some adult seals were more likely than others to become foster parents. For one thing, all the foster parents were female. That's perhaps unsurprising, since part of what drives orphans to seek out care is the need to nurse. Yet among females, the most common foster seal was a mother who had lost her own pup. Why might this be? One possible reason is that fostering helped these females reproduce later on. Regular nursing may induce ovulation, which in turn could make a female more likely to give birth to her own pup the following season. The evidence supporting this explanation is tenuous, but the hypothesis is at least reasonable.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another possibility is that mothers are behaviourally and physiologically prepared to care for their pups immediately following birth. Given the absence of their own young, the motivation towards maternal care may be so great that they redirect their attention onto other, unrelated pups. &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_C._Williams\"&gt;Biologist George C Williams&lt;/a&gt; called this phenomenon \"misplaced reproductive function&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One other common form of adoption occurred when a female who had never given birth still cared for an unrelated infant. Riedman speculated that those females might gain valuable maternal experience, increasing their own parenting competence. So perhaps there is something in it for foster parents after all.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01tqlts\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Elephant seal pups aren't the only ones to win adoptive parents either. It's actually fairly common among birds. Many chicks intentionally abandon the nests in which they hatched to seek out temporary or full adoption by foster parents. This \"nest switching\" has been seen in seabirds like gulls and terns, as well as storks, raptors, egrets and herons.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Take white storks. In one breeding season, biologists from Spain's Universidad de Cordoba found nest switching in 40% of broods across three distinct white-stork breeding colonies. That &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0003347285701388\"&gt;infant birds seek out new digs&lt;/a&gt; actually makes sense, since they could benefit from a longer period of parental care. If they moved into nests containing fewer or younger chicks than their previous homes, then they could also receive more food by more easily outcompeting smaller adoptive siblings.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But why would the adoptive parents allow the intruder into their nest, especially to the detriment of their own young? It could simply be that evolution has not endowed the parents with the ability to discriminate their own chicks from strangers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This lack of discriminatory ability is seen particularly starkly in &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0003347298909137\"&gt;Lake Erie's ring-billed gulls&lt;/a&gt;. Nest invasions are common, but unlike for white storks, there is a significant cost incurred by the foster parents: half as many of their own chicks grow to fledging age than the gulls that did not adopt.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01tqn19\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Given such high risks for adoption, why hasn't evolution endowed these birds with a better ability to identify &ndash; and reject &ndash; intruders? Biologist Kevin Brown of York University thinks that the costs of better chick discrimination could be even higher. \"If the cost of rejecting one&rsquo;s own offspring is greater than accepting an alien chick,\" he writes &lt;a href=\"ftp://psyftp.mcmaster.ca/dalywilson/Papers/infanticide/gullAdopt.pdf\"&gt;in the journal Animal Behaviour&lt;/a&gt;, \"selection will favour universal acceptance of young in the nest.\" In other words, it may be better to needlessly waste resources on alien infiltrators than to accidentally reject one of your own hatchlings.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Adoption is also fairly common among non-human primates. It's been seen in &lt;a href=\"http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/BF02381155\"&gt;red howler monkeys in Venezuela&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\"http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10329-007-0066-x\"&gt;black-fronted titi monkeys&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\"http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10329-007-0066-x\"&gt;woolly spider monkeys&lt;/a&gt; in Brazil, and even among chimpanzees. But in all of those cases the adoption has always been within species. Storks adopt stork chicks, howler monkeys adopt infant howlers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In captivity, there are occasionally cross-species adoptions, such as between rhesus and Japanese macaques or among &lt;a href=\"http://www.scielo.br/scielo.php?pid=S0034-71081998000400014&amp;script=sci_arttext\"&gt;different types of marmoset&lt;/a&gt;. But these species are closely related, and have similar behaviour.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Given that, perhaps &lt;a href=\"http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1002/ajp.20259/abstract\"&gt;the most striking instance of adoption in the wild&lt;/a&gt; was between completely different types of monkeys. In 2004, researchers noticed an infant marmoset travelling with a group of capuchin monkeys at the &lt;a href=\"http://www.greenwingvalley.com/\"&gt;Green Wing Valley&lt;/a&gt; wildlife reserve in Brazil. For at least 14 months, the marmoset was raised by its adoptive capuchin group, alternating between two primary female foster mothers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01tqp6z\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One reason the adoption was so surprising is because marmosets and capuchins are so different. For one thing, a fully-grown capuchin weighs perhaps 3-4kg (7-8lbs), but a fully-grown marmoset maxes out at less than 500g (1lb). The two species also have different feeding patterns and different parenting styles.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Despite those differences, the juvenile marmoset became wholly integrated into its adoptive social group. \"It travelled and fed with the group, responded to alarm vocalisations given by members of the group, and played,\" the researchers &lt;a href=\"http://www.ip.usp.br/ethocebus/arquivos/AJP_Izar&amp;al_2006.pdf\"&gt;wrote in the American Journal of Primatology&lt;/a&gt;. During social play with their unlikely friend, the juvenile capuchins actually adjusted the force of their movements to account for the puny marmoset's size and strength. And the adult capuchins, including the dominant male, were extremely tolerant of the impostor. The marmoset would patiently watch the adults crack nuts between two rocks and sneak an occasional snack, much as a young capuchin would.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But he (or she) wasn't simply a marmoset in a capuchin suit. Capuchins travel by leaping from tree to tree. Given its size, the marmoset often struggled to keep up. The capuchins all but ignored the marmoset's distress cries, despite being within hearing distance. Like many human adoptions, the match wasn't always perfect.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;How did it happen? It's likely that the adult capuchins were simply predisposed to care for young primates. They're also extremely tolerant of infants in the first place. In addition, given how small the marmoset is compared to the capuchins, they didn't need to give up too much of their own food so that it might survive. A female capuchin would barely notice a tiny marmoset clinging to her fur, making it reasonable to assume that the infant didn't slow her down at all.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It seems as if the drive to care for helpless infants is fairly universal among species that care for their own young &ndash; and even between different animals. What else could explain our own species' obsession with puppies, kittens and other &lt;a href=\"http://www.zooborns.com/\"&gt;baby animals&lt;/a&gt;?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-03-12T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Why do animals adopt?","HeadlineShort":"Why do animals adopt?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"In the animal kingdom, parents adopt babies that arent their own, and even other species, says Jason G Goldman. Why do they do it?","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"In the animal kingdom, parents adopt babies that arent their own, and even other species, says Jason G Goldman. Why do they do it?","SummaryShort":"The surprising care of alien babies","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"82ed88c3-289c-4e79-a17e-47102f86761a","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140312-why-do-animals-adopt","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140312-why-do-animals-adopt"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140312-why-do-animals-adopt","_id":"559a6a4382f2d2dbf7bd4dc7"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Of all the world&rsquo;s materials, which one will &ldquo;run out&rdquo; first? The more we consume as a society, the more we hear about how vital ores and minerals are dwindling, so it seems logical to assume that a few may be about to disappear.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet that may be entirely the wrong way of looking at the problem. According to natural resources experts, many of the materials we rely upon in modern life won&rsquo;t &ldquo;run out&rdquo; at all. Unfortunately, the scenario they paint about what will happen instead in the near future is hardly rosy either.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Some of our most cherished devices &ndash; smartphones, computers and medical equipment, for instance &ndash; rely on a rich list of elemental ingredients. Mobile phones alone contain a whopping 60 to 64 elements. &ldquo;Many of these metals are present in only minute amounts, a milligramme or less,&rdquo; says Armin Reller, a chemist and the chair of resource strategy at Augsburg University in Germany. &ldquo;But they are very important for the function of the device.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This includes things like copper, aluminum and iron, but also less well-known materials, like the &ldquo;rare earth elements&rdquo;, what the &lt;a href=\"http://www.rareelementresources.com/rare-earth-elements#.UyG2dEJdW5I\"&gt;Japanese refer to&lt;/a&gt; as &ldquo;the seeds of technology&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01v2zbs\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The latter class of materials has come under particular scrutiny because they&rsquo;re a vital ingredient in smartphones, hybrid cars, wind turbines, computers and more. China &ndash; which produces around 90% of the world&rsquo;s rare earth metals &ndash; claims that its &lt;a href=\"http://www.bloomberg.com/news/2010-10-16/china-says-its-medium-heavy-rare-earth-reserves-may-last-only-15-20-years.html\"&gt;mines might run dry in just 15-20 years&lt;/a&gt;. Likewise, if demand continues for &lt;a href=\"http://www.science.org.au/nova/newscientist/ns_diagrams/027ns_005image2.jpg\"&gt;indium&lt;/a&gt;, some say it will be gone in about 10 years; platinum in 15 years; and silver in 20 years. Looking farther into the future, other sources claim that things like aluminum might run dry &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120618-global-resources-stock-check\"&gt;in about 80 years&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.physik.uni-augsburg.de/lehrstuehle/rst/downloads/Materials_Handbook_Rev_2012.pdf\"&gt;Other studies&lt;/a&gt; indicate that rhodium, followed by gold, platinum and tellurium, are some of the rarest elements in terms of their percentage in the planet&rsquo;s crust and their importance to society.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As startling as these figures sound, however, the complete loss of silver, platinum, aluminum or any other mineral resource will likely never come to pass, according to Thomas Graedel, director of the Center for Industrial Ecology at the Yale School of Forestry and Environmental Studies. We have never completely run out of a natural resource, he says, and we almost certainly never will.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Supply and demand&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thinking in terms of &ldquo;running out&rdquo; is not the right way to approach this problem. For starters, it&rsquo;s impossible to prove a negative. Scientists could never definitively say &ldquo;The world has no more silver reserves&rdquo; without checking every last subterranean nook and cranny on the planet. The more practical reason, however, is that by the time we reached the point that running out is even a consideration, the price tag for those last remnants would be prohibitively expensive, and manufacturers would not be able to turn a profit on any products made from them. They already would have moved on to a substitute resource &ndash; even an inferior one. &ldquo;As supply and demand change, prices change and people adapt what they need and how they use it,&rdquo; says Lawrence Meinert, program coordinator of the US Geological Survey&rsquo;s Mineral Resources Program. &ldquo;This means that you can never consume everything because the price gets so high you stop using it.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the 1980s, &lt;a href=\"http://www.slate.com/articles/news_and_politics/explainer/2010/10/has_the_earth_run_out_of_any_natural_resources.html\"&gt;mining for cryolite&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; used in processing aluminum ore &ndash; stopped because remaining reserves were too small to justify the expense of mining. We simply moved on to using a synthetic substitute.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s not really possible to put a label on what the world&rsquo;s rarest resource is, either. Instead of thinking in terms of how many tonnes of element X or mineral Y are left in the earth, Meinert says, rarity depends on how easy it is to get our hands on that resource, and also on the market&rsquo;s demand for that commodity. Rarity, thus, becomes more a question of availability than of actual physical reserves, and that availability can be influenced by a variety of forces.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01v2xzz\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For starters, some resources such as indium &ndash; found in computer and smartphone display screens &ndash; are byproducts of other mining operations. Almost all of the world&rsquo;s indium comes from zinc mines; there are no dedicated indium mines, because it occurs in such small amounts mining for it is impractical. So if demand for zinc declines &ndash; say, because car manufacturers switch from steel to aluminum bodies, as they have been doing recently &ndash; then this would have an impact on indium. &ldquo;Nature puts deposits where it feels like, without worrying about whether we have the energy or water handy to deal with it,&rdquo; says Graedel.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In other cases, demand might surpass supply, even if there are dedicated mines for the material. Certain resources are sometimes referred to by the German term Gew&uuml;rzmetall &ndash; &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://www.ingentaconnect.com/content/oekom/gaia/2009/00000018/00000002/art00010\"&gt;spice metal&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; because, like a dash of cinnamon or saffron tying a dish&rsquo;s flavour together, these materials compose only a small proportion of an electronic device&rsquo;s makeup but are essential for that device to function. Palladium, which is &lt;a href=\"http://www.lenntech.com/periodic/elements/pd.htm\"&gt;used as a capacitor&lt;/a&gt; in cell phones, makes up just 0.015% of those devices&rsquo; composition, but the cell phone industry goes through about 15 tonnes of that rare metal each year. Given that palladium is also used by the automotive, jewellery, dental, health and electronics industries, eventually, competition could drive up prices, and supply could fall short.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Politics can come into play, too. Most of &lt;a href=\"http://pubs.usgs.gov/of/2011/1042/\"&gt;the rare-earth-metals supply&lt;/a&gt; comes from mines in China. Several years ago, China decided to &lt;a href=\"http://www.economist.com/node/16944034\"&gt;cut down on its export&lt;/a&gt; of those elements. As a result, the price of rare earth metals &ndash; and the goods they are used to make &ndash; &lt;a href=\"https://theconversation.com/metals-in-your-smartphone-have-no-substitutes-21142\"&gt;has increased&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s not that the rest of the world does not have rare earth metals,&rdquo; Meinert says. The US, for example, holds around 38% of the world&rsquo;s deposits, but &lt;a href=\"http://minerals.cr.usgs.gov/projects/Mountain_Pass_carbonatite/index.html\"&gt;only one mine&lt;/a&gt; in southern California (which was closed for several years due to competition from China) is currently producing them. &ldquo;It takes a long time to put a mine into production,&rdquo; Meinert says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In addition to countries setting their own embargoes on exports, conflicts have an impact on resource availability. Fighting in the Democratic Republic of the Congo could affect the world&rsquo;s supply of coltan, for example, a metallic ore used to make capacitors.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;No substitute&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So should we worry about dwindling resources? Historically, the argument that, if one element or another runs dry, the world will find alternatives has worked pretty well. Unfortunately, however, in today&rsquo;s increasingly complex world, good substitutes are hard to come by. In &lt;a href=\"http://www.pnas.org/content/early/2013/11/27/1312752110\"&gt;a recent study&lt;/a&gt;, Graedel and his colleagues tried to identify the best substitutes for 62 metals. Twelve of those metals, they found, had no substitute at all for their major uses, and none of the 62 they studied had a substitute available to cover all of their uses. &ldquo;Because we chose what&rsquo;s almost always the best from a long list of materials, performance is likely to decrease &ndash; computers will get slower, engines will be less efficient and so on &ndash; if we start making substitutions,&rdquo; Graedel says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Within the next several years Graedel hopes to have a better idea of which metals might soon be in short supply. Product development teams could begin thinking of possible substitutes, and to focus on developing devices using materials with more reliable supplies.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01v2ysq\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the meantime, it would help if consumers found responsible ways of &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20140218-why-your-old-tech-holds-treasure\"&gt;recycling their used electronics&lt;/a&gt;. In 2009, &lt;a href=\"http://www.epa.gov/epawaste/conserve/materials/ecycling/faq.htm\"&gt;US citizens recycled&lt;/a&gt; just 25% of their used TVs and computers, while as little as 8% of mobile phones were recycled. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s really a tragedy that we spend all this time and effort getting gold and platinum and other things we use in our electronics, but then we use them once and don&rsquo;t worry about what happens to them after that,&rdquo; Graedel says. &ldquo;Recycling won&rsquo;t solve the problem [of scarcity], but it certainly would help.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If the world began recycling in full force and combined those efforts with new technologies for efficiently finding, extracting and sharing the resources we need, it could be possible that the materials integral to modern life will last for generations to come, Reller says. &ldquo;But that hope might be a little bit dream-like,&rdquo; he admits.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The balance of supply and demand does affect us, especially when it comes to the price and availability of the technology in our lives. So while no material is so scarce that it is about to disappear entirely &ndash; we should worry that many could soon become out of reach.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-03-18T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"What is the worlds scarcest material?","HeadlineShort":"The worlds scarcest material","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Should we worry about natural resources running out? As Rachel Nuwer discovers, thats the wrong way of looking at a far more complex problem.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Should we worry about natural resources running out? As Rachel Nuwer discovers, thats the wrong way of looking at a far more complex problem","SummaryShort":"Can natural resources totally run out?","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:17Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"1d3fb4dc-3970-4bbb-9e10-bc9c3ede0321","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140314-the-worlds-scarcest-material","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:17Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140314-the-worlds-scarcest-material"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140314-the-worlds-scarcest-material","_id":"559a6a4382f2d2dbf7bd4dd5"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;In the Atacama Desert in northern Chile, it looks as if nothing could ever survive. It is one of the driest places in the world, and some sections of the Mars-like expanse can go 50 years without feeling a drop of rain. As poet Alonso de Ercilla put it in 1569: &ldquo;Towards Atacama, near the deserted coast, you see a land without men, where there is not a bird, not a beast, nor a tree, nor any vegetation.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet Atacama is not devoid of life. Microorganisms called endoliths have found a way to cling on, by hiding themselves inside the pores of rocks, where there&rsquo;s just enough water to survive. &ldquo;They support a whole community of organisms that eat the byproducts of their metabolism,&rdquo; says Jocelyne DiRuggiero, a microbiologist at Johns Hopkins University. &ldquo;And they&rsquo;re all just sitting right there in the rocks &ndash; it&rsquo;s quite fascinating.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Life, it seems, has an incredible knack for finding ways to persist. Indeed, microorganisms have been around for nearly four billion years, giving them ample time to adapt to some of the most extreme conditions in the natural world. But are there places left on Earth so harsh that they are rendered sterile?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t23kc\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Heat is a good starting point for answering this question. The record for heat tolerance is currently held by a group of organisms called hyperthermophile methanogens, which thrive around the edges of hydrothermal vents in the deep sea. Some of these organisms can grow at &lt;a href=\"http://www.pnas.org/content/105/31/10949.full.pdf\"&gt;temperatures of up to 122C&lt;/a&gt; (252F).&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Most researchers believe that around 150C (302F) is the theoretical cut-off point for life, however. At that temperature proteins fall apart and chemical reactions cannot occur &ndash; a quirk of the biochemistry that life on Earth (so far as we know) abides by. This means that microorganisms can thrive around hydrothermal vents, but not directly within them, where temperatures can reach up to 464C (867F). The same is true for the interior of an active volcano on land. &ldquo;I really think temperature is the most hostile parameter,&rdquo; says Helena Santos, a microbial physiologist at the New University of Lisbon and president of the &lt;a href=\"http://extremophiles.org/\"&gt;International Society for Extremophiles&lt;/a&gt;. When things get hot enough, she says, &ldquo;It&rsquo;s impossible &ndash; everything is destroyed.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t23bt\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;High pressure, by contrast, appears to be less of a problem for life. This means that heat rather than depth probably limits how far below the surface of the Earth life occurs. The centre of the Earth&rsquo;s 6,000C (10,800F) temperature certainly precludes all life, although the depth at which the cut-off occurs is still under investigation. One microorganism called &lt;em&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencemag.org/content/322/5899/275\"&gt;Desulforudis audaxviator&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/em&gt; was discovered nearly two miles (3.2km) below the Earth&rsquo;s surface, in a South African gold mine. It has not been in contact with the surface for potentially millions of years, and survives by siphoning nutrients from rocks undergoing radioactive decay.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Life exists at the other extreme, too, in subfreezing conditions. Bacteria in the genus &lt;em&gt;Psychrobacter &lt;/em&gt;can happily live below -10C (14F) in Siberian permafrost and Antarctic glacier mud. &lt;a href=\"http://www.nytimes.com/2013/02/07/science/living-bacteria-found-deep-under-antarctic-ice-scientists-say.html?_r=0\"&gt;Living cells recently turned up&lt;/a&gt; in a subglacial lake below the Antarctic ice. And Antarctica&rsquo;s hypersaline Deep Lake hosts unique &lt;a href=\"http://www.pnas.org/content/early/2013/09/25/1307090110.abstract\"&gt;salt-loving species&lt;/a&gt;, even at -20C (-4F). To survive in these environments, microrganisms possess features such as specially adapted membranes and protein structures, and anti-freeze molecules within their cells. Given that the Earth has been covered in ice multiple times since life first evolved, &ldquo;an ice-covered lake in Antarctica does not seem all that extreme,&rdquo; says Jill Mikucki, a microbiologist at the University of Tennessee.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t23nt\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Radiation typically does not deter microorganisms, either. So long as they are not in the direct pathway of an atomic blast &ndash; which would likely burn them up &ndash; they can thrive in containers of radioactive waste or near the epicentre of the Chernobyl disaster, for example. &lt;em&gt;Deinococcus radiodurans&lt;/em&gt;, one of the hardiest of the radio-resistant microorganisms, has &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0378109702009539\"&gt;survived trips into space&lt;/a&gt; and can endure radiation doses of up to 15,000 gray (the standard measure of the absorbed dose of radiation). For humans, just 5 gray causes death.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Likewise, what we register as deadly chemical environments, some extremophiles call home sweet home. Various organisms depend on arsenic, mercury or other heavy metals for their growth and survival, while others prefer cyanide. In &lt;a href=\"http://www.exploratorium.edu/kamchatka/\"&gt;the hot springs of Kamchatka in Russia&lt;/a&gt;, menageries of microorganisms metabolise using sulphur or carbon monoxide. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s hard to find a chemical that can kill all life,&rdquo; says Frank Robb, a microbiologist at the University of Maryland.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There might be isolated exceptions, however. Don Juan Pond in Antarctica is the saltiest known body of water on the planet, with salinity levels topping 40%. (The Dead Sea is around 33%). Researchers have recovered traces of microbial life from the pond, but they are still trying to determine whether it is actively growing and replicating there, or just blowing in from other locations. Don Juan counts as &ldquo;an example of a place on the surface of Earth where we might expect life, but cannot verify the presence of active life,&rdquo; says Corien Bakermans, a microbiologist at Penn State University.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For now, extreme heat and some synthetic laboratory environments might be the only sterile conditions on the planet&rsquo;s surface that it&rsquo;s possible to find zero traces of life. New organisms are regularly discovered that push the boundaries of life as we understand it, although where that line will ultimately be drawn remains unknown. As Santos says, &ldquo;What does not exist is more difficult to prove than what does exist.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even if there are some lone holdouts of sterility in the natural world, however, the environment exists in a constant state of flux &ndash; and if extremophiles teach us anything, it is that organisms are always capable of adapting. &ldquo;Give them enough time and they will find a way,&rdquo; says DiRuggiero.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-03-03T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"The last place on Earth without life","HeadlineShort":"Last place on Earth without life","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Weird and wonderful creatures can thrive in the most hostile parts on the planet, but there are a few places too harsh for even the hardiest, discovers Rachel Nuwer. ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"Weird and wonderful creatures thrive in the planets most hostile places, but there are a few spots too harsh for even the hardiest, discovers Rachel Nuwer","SummaryShort":"Where even the hardiest creatures perish","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:29Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"84ebbff6-bef6-488c-8664-566a2fd8f89e","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140303-last-place-on-earth-without-life","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:29Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140303-last-place-on-earth-without-life"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140303-last-place-on-earth-without-life","_id":"559a6a4282f2d2dbf7bd4dad"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"ib2video","AssetVideoIb2":[],"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":null,"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;They are nature&rsquo;s most opportunistic scavengers, soaring effortlessly in the air on the lookout for their next meal. Mankind has often treated these birds with disgust, but recently it&rsquo;s been revealed how much we owe them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Vultures feed on the carcasses of dead animals, helping lessen the chance of disease outbreaks &ndash; a fact that was starkly revealed in India over the last few decades. Widespread use of a drug to treat livestock ended up poisoning the birds. &ldquo;We think we&rsquo;ve lost somewhere around 40 million birds in the space of two decades, it&rsquo;s probably the biggest population crash that has ever happened,&rdquo; says Jemima Parry-Jones, director of the &lt;a href=\"http://www.icbp.org/index/\"&gt;International Centre for Birds of Prey&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In this film, Parry Jones, Dr Ananya Mukherjee of the &lt;a href=\"http://www.save-vultures.org/\"&gt;Saving Asian Vultures from Extinction&lt;/a&gt; (Save), Dr M Sanjayan of &lt;a href=\"http://www.nature.org/\"&gt;The Nature Conservancy&lt;/a&gt; and environmental economist Pavan Sukhdev reveal what happened next. Without the vultures, carcasses rotted, creating a breeding ground for diseases and leaving a terrible stench. Feral dogs thrived, bringing with them a rise in rabies; India now has the highest number of rabies cases in the world.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now livestock are being treated with a drug that doesn&rsquo;t harm vultures, in the hope that the population will recover so that they can return to their vitally important role. As Parry-Jones says: &ldquo;People tend to think they&rsquo;re ugly, dirty and smelly, and they&rsquo;re far from it and they&rsquo;re absolutely crucial to the environment. They&rsquo;re the only dustmen in the world who&rsquo;ve never gone on strike.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this video, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-02-11T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Vultures: Natures rubbish collectors who never strike","HeadlineShort":"Vultures: Halting killer diseases","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Vultures are often derided for being ugly and smelly, but these incredibly efficient scavengers help humanity by eating dead animals.
