Earth Unplugged reveals the worlds most impressive animal mums.\n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"&lt;iframe width=\"624\" height=\"351\" src=\"//www.youtube.com/embed/EFIPrFGOj2Y?rel=0\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen&gt;&lt;/iframe&gt;","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"customEmbed","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":null,"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Do you know which animal makes a &ldquo;boom&rdquo; sound? Or which one can imitate a chainsaw? Find out as Maddie Moate brings us 10 of the weirdest and most impressive noises from nature.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;For more videos subscribe to the &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/user/EarthUnpluggedTV?feature=watch\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Earth Unplugged channel on YouTube&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;. &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this video or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-02-08T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Top 10 amazing animal sounds","HeadlineShort":"Top 10 amazing animal sounds","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"From a boom to a chainsaw buzz, animals make some extraordinary sounds. Earth Unplugged reveals 10 of the weirdest and most impressive noises from nature.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"video","SummaryLong":"From a boom to a chainsaw buzz, animals make some extraordinary sounds. Earth Unplugged reveals 10 of the weirdest and most impressive noises from nature.","SummaryShort":"From booms to a chainsaw buzz","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"0dfff842-614f-4259-91bb-925344d342ce","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140207-top-10-amazing-animal-sounds","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140207-top-10-amazing-animal-sounds"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140207-top-10-amazing-animal-sounds","_id":"559a6a4082f2d2dbf7bd4d61"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Is adopting a child a benevolent or a foolish act? If you were taking a cold evolutionary perspective, it would appear to be the latter.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For foster parents, there are huge costs involved, with no promise of passing on genes. Scientists have long been interested in adoption, because it seems to be wholly altruistic. But this makes it especially perplexing in animals, who do not have the cultural influences we do. So could taking a closer look at adoption in non-humans shed any light on why it&rsquo;s so common?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of the more striking places to see adoption in the animal kingdom is Ano Nuevo Island, rising from the sea less than one kilometre off the rocky California coast. Once a year, it is host to the breeding of hundreds of northern elephant seals.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;From 1976 onwards, marine scientist Marianne Riedman, together with her colleague Burney Le Boeuf, studied adoption among the seals &ndash; and why it was happening. It's a crowded beach, with bad weather, high tides and rough surf, which perhaps explains why one-quarter to two-thirds of pups each year were separated from their mothers at least once &ndash; some permanently.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01tqlkp\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The researchers counted a total of 572 orphaned pups over the course of the four consecutive breeding seasons. Riedman charmingly &lt;a href=\"http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/BF00300063\"&gt;referred to the ones that were adopted as \"alien young\"&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Intriguingly, some adult seals were more likely than others to become foster parents. For one thing, all the foster parents were female. That's perhaps unsurprising, since part of what drives orphans to seek out care is the need to nurse. Yet among females, the most common foster seal was a mother who had lost her own pup. Why might this be? One possible reason is that fostering helped these females reproduce later on. Regular nursing may induce ovulation, which in turn could make a female more likely to give birth to her own pup the following season. The evidence supporting this explanation is tenuous, but the hypothesis is at least reasonable.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another possibility is that mothers are behaviourally and physiologically prepared to care for their pups immediately following birth. Given the absence of their own young, the motivation towards maternal care may be so great that they redirect their attention onto other, unrelated pups. &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_C._Williams\"&gt;Biologist George C Williams&lt;/a&gt; called this phenomenon \"misplaced reproductive function&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One other common form of adoption occurred when a female who had never given birth still cared for an unrelated infant. Riedman speculated that those females might gain valuable maternal experience, increasing their own parenting competence. So perhaps there is something in it for foster parents after all.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01tqlts\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Elephant seal pups aren't the only ones to win adoptive parents either. It's actually fairly common among birds. Many chicks intentionally abandon the nests in which they hatched to seek out temporary or full adoption by foster parents. This \"nest switching\" has been seen in seabirds like gulls and terns, as well as storks, raptors, egrets and herons.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Take white storks. In one breeding season, biologists from Spain's Universidad de Cordoba found nest switching in 40% of broods across three distinct white-stork breeding colonies. That &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0003347285701388\"&gt;infant birds seek out new digs&lt;/a&gt; actually makes sense, since they could benefit from a longer period of parental care. If they moved into nests containing fewer or younger chicks than their previous homes, then they could also receive more food by more easily outcompeting smaller adoptive siblings.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But why would the adoptive parents allow the intruder into their nest, especially to the detriment of their own young? It could simply be that evolution has not endowed the parents with the ability to discriminate their own chicks from strangers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This lack of discriminatory ability is seen particularly starkly in &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0003347298909137\"&gt;Lake Erie's ring-billed gulls&lt;/a&gt;. Nest invasions are common, but unlike for white storks, there is a significant cost incurred by the foster parents: half as many of their own chicks grow to fledging age than the gulls that did not adopt.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01tqn19\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Given such high risks for adoption, why hasn't evolution endowed these birds with a better ability to identify &ndash; and reject &ndash; intruders? Biologist Kevin Brown of York University thinks that the costs of better chick discrimination could be even higher. \"If the cost of rejecting one&rsquo;s own offspring is greater than accepting an alien chick,\" he writes &lt;a href=\"ftp://psyftp.mcmaster.ca/dalywilson/Papers/infanticide/gullAdopt.pdf\"&gt;in the journal Animal Behaviour&lt;/a&gt;, \"selection will favour universal acceptance of young in the nest.\" In other words, it may be better to needlessly waste resources on alien infiltrators than to accidentally reject one of your own hatchlings.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Adoption is also fairly common among non-human primates. It's been seen in &lt;a href=\"http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/BF02381155\"&gt;red howler monkeys in Venezuela&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\"http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10329-007-0066-x\"&gt;black-fronted titi monkeys&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\"http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10329-007-0066-x\"&gt;woolly spider monkeys&lt;/a&gt; in Brazil, and even among chimpanzees. But in all of those cases the adoption has always been within species. Storks adopt stork chicks, howler monkeys adopt infant howlers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In captivity, there are occasionally cross-species adoptions, such as between rhesus and Japanese macaques or among &lt;a href=\"http://www.scielo.br/scielo.php?pid=S0034-71081998000400014&amp;script=sci_arttext\"&gt;different types of marmoset&lt;/a&gt;. But these species are closely related, and have similar behaviour.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Given that, perhaps &lt;a href=\"http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1002/ajp.20259/abstract\"&gt;the most striking instance of adoption in the wild&lt;/a&gt; was between completely different types of monkeys. In 2004, researchers noticed an infant marmoset travelling with a group of capuchin monkeys at the &lt;a href=\"http://www.greenwingvalley.com/\"&gt;Green Wing Valley&lt;/a&gt; wildlife reserve in Brazil. For at least 14 months, the marmoset was raised by its adoptive capuchin group, alternating between two primary female foster mothers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01tqp6z\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One reason the adoption was so surprising is because marmosets and capuchins are so different. For one thing, a fully-grown capuchin weighs perhaps 3-4kg (7-8lbs), but a fully-grown marmoset maxes out at less than 500g (1lb). The two species also have different feeding patterns and different parenting styles.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Despite those differences, the juvenile marmoset became wholly integrated into its adoptive social group. \"It travelled and fed with the group, responded to alarm vocalisations given by members of the group, and played,\" the researchers &lt;a href=\"http://www.ip.usp.br/ethocebus/arquivos/AJP_Izar&amp;al_2006.pdf\"&gt;wrote in the American Journal of Primatology&lt;/a&gt;. During social play with their unlikely friend, the juvenile capuchins actually adjusted the force of their movements to account for the puny marmoset's size and strength. And the adult capuchins, including the dominant male, were extremely tolerant of the impostor. The marmoset would patiently watch the adults crack nuts between two rocks and sneak an occasional snack, much as a young capuchin would.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But he (or she) wasn't simply a marmoset in a capuchin suit. Capuchins travel by leaping from tree to tree. Given its size, the marmoset often struggled to keep up. The capuchins all but ignored the marmoset's distress cries, despite being within hearing distance. Like many human adoptions, the match wasn't always perfect.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;How did it happen? It's likely that the adult capuchins were simply predisposed to care for young primates. They're also extremely tolerant of infants in the first place. In addition, given how small the marmoset is compared to the capuchins, they didn't need to give up too much of their own food so that it might survive. A female capuchin would barely notice a tiny marmoset clinging to her fur, making it reasonable to assume that the infant didn't slow her down at all.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It seems as if the drive to care for helpless infants is fairly universal among species that care for their own young &ndash; and even between different animals. What else could explain our own species' obsession with puppies, kittens and other &lt;a href=\"http://www.zooborns.com/\"&gt;baby animals&lt;/a&gt;?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-03-12T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Why do animals adopt?","HeadlineShort":"Why do animals adopt?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"In the animal kingdom, parents adopt babies that arent their own, and even other species, says Jason G Goldman. villagers are the nation's foot soldiers in the fight against drug-resistant malaria. (Getty Images)","SynopsisMedium":"Cambodian villagers are the nation's foot soldiers in the fight against drug-resistant malaria. (Getty Images)","SynopsisShort":"(Getty Images)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1w/67/p01w67qd.jpg","Title":"Malaria_Cambodia_Getty.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01w67qd","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01w67qd","_id":"559a68ab82f2d2dbf7bd124b"},{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":192066,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":549,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/1w/6b/p01w6bs4.jpg","SourceWidth":976,"SynopsisLong":"Spraying insecticide in villages is one way to eliminate malarial mosquitoes (Science Photo Library)","SynopsisMedium":"Spraying insecticide in villages is one way to eliminate malarial mosquitoes (Science Photo Library)","SynopsisShort":"(Science Photo Library)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1w/6b/p01w6bs4.jpg","Title":"control3_SPL_crop.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01w6bs4","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01w6bs4","_id":"559a68ab82f2d2dbf7bd124f"},{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":109335,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":549,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/1w/6c/p01w6c33.jpg","SourceWidth":976,"SynopsisLong":"Blood samples dry during an intense malaria screening along the Thai-Cambodian border (Getty Images)","SynopsisMedium":"Blood samples dry during an intense malaria screening along the Thai-Cambodian border (Getty Images)","SynopsisShort":"(Getty Images)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1w/6c/p01w6c33.jpg","Title":"Malaria_BloodTests_Getty.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01w6c33","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01w6c33","_id":"559a68ab82f2d2dbf7bd1250"}],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Description":"&lt;p&gt;Ed\nis an award-winning science author. He writes the blog &lt;a href=\"http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/notrocketscience/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Not\nExactly Rocket Science&lt;/a&gt; and his work has appeared in New Scientist, Nature,\nScientific American, the Guardian, the Times, Wired UK, Discover and more. He\ntweets at &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/edyong209\"&gt;@edyong209&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Ed Yong","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:29Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"520648c1-727e-4c71-9672-6b662e851fa2","Id":"wwfuture/author/ed-yong","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:29Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"ed-yong"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/ed-yong","_id":"559a668b82f2d2dbf7bcb5f1"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;The meandering Moei river marks the natural boundary between Thailand and Myanmar. Its muddy waters are at their fullest, but Fran&ccedil;ois Nosten still crosses them in just a minute, aboard a narrow, wooden boat. In the dry season, he could wade across. As he steps onto the western riverbank, in Myanmar, he passes no checkpoint and presents no passport.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The air is cool. After months of rain, the surrounding jungle pops with vivid lime and emerald hues. Nosten climbs a set of wooden slats that wind away from the bank, up a muddy slope. His pace, as ever, seems relaxed and out of kilter with his almost permanently grave expression and urgent purpose. Nosten, a rangy Frenchman with tousled brown hair and glasses, is one of the world&rsquo;s leading experts on malaria. He is here to avert a looming disaster. At the top of the slope, he reaches a small village of simple wooden buildings with tin and thatch roofs. This is Hka Naw Tah, home to around 400 people and a testing ground for Nosten&rsquo;s bold plan to completely stamp out malaria from this critical corner of the world.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Malaria is the work of the single-celled &lt;em&gt;Plasmodium&lt;/em&gt; parasites, with &lt;em&gt;Plasmodium falciparum&lt;/em&gt; being chief among them. They spread between people through the bites of mosquitoes, invading first the liver, then the red blood cells. The first symptoms are generic and flu-like: fever, headache, sweats and chills, vomiting. At that point, the immune system usually curtails the infection. But if the parasites spread to the kidneys, lungs and brain, things go downhill quickly. Organs start failing. Infected red blood cells clog the brain&rsquo;s blood vessels, depriving it of oxygen and leading to seizures, unconsciousness and death.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w62tp\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When Nosten first arrived in south-east Asia almost 30 years ago, malaria was the biggest killer in the region. Artemisinin changed everything. Spectacularly fast and effective, the drug arrived on the scene in 1994, when options for treating malaria were running out. Since then, &ldquo;cases have just gone down, down, down,&rdquo; says Nosten. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve never seen so few in the rainy season &ndash; a few hundred this year compared to tens of thousands before.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But he has no time for celebration. Artemisinin used to clear &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;in a day; now it can take several. The parasite has started to become resistant. The wonder drug is failing. It is the latest reprise of a decades-long theme: we attack malaria with a new drug, it mounts an evolutionary riposte.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Back in his office, Nosten pulls up a map showing the current whereabouts of the resistant parasites. Three coloured bands highlight the borders between Cambodia and Vietnam, Cambodia and Thailand, and Thailand and Myanmar (Burma). Borders. Bold lines on maps, but invisible in reality. A river that can be crossed in a rickety boat is no barrier to a parasite that rides in the salivary glands of mosquitoes or the red blood cells of humans.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;History tells us what happens next. Over the last century, almost every frontline antimalarial drug &ndash; chloroquine, sulfadoxine, pyrimethamine &ndash; has become obsolete because of defiant parasites that emerged from western Cambodia. From this cradle of resistance, the parasites gradually spread west to Africa, causing the deaths of millions. Malaria already kills around 660,000 people every year, and most of them are African kids. If artemisinin resistance reached that continent, it would be catastrophic, especially since there are no good replacement drugs on the immediate horizon.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w632f\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Nosten thinks that without radical measures, resistance will spread to India and Bangladesh. Once that happens, it will be too late. Those countries are too big, too populous, too uneven in their health services to even dream about containing the resistant parasites. Once there, they will inevitably spread further. He thinks it will happen in three years, maybe four. &ldquo;Look at the speed of change on this border. It&rsquo;s exponential. It&rsquo;s not going to take 10 or 15 years to reach Bangladesh. It&rsquo;ll take just a few. We have to do something before it&rsquo;s too late.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hundreds of scientists are developing innovative new ways of dealing with malaria, from potential vaccines to new drugs, genetically modified mosquitoes to lethal fungi. As Nosten sees it, none of these will be ready in time. The only way of stopping artemisinin resistance, he says, is to completely remove malaria from its cradle of resistance. &ldquo;If you want to eliminate artemisinin resistance, you have to eliminate malaria,&rdquo; says Nosten. Not control it, not contain it. Eliminate it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That makes the Moei river more than a border between nations. It&rsquo;s Stalingrad. It&rsquo;s Thermopylae. It&rsquo;s the last chance for halting the creeping obsolescence of our best remaining drug. What happens here will decide the fate of millions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Weapon failure&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;The world tried to eliminate malaria 60 years ago. Malaria was a global affliction back then, infecting hundreds of thousands of troops during World War II. This helped motivate a swell of postwar research. To fight the disease, in 1946 the United States created what is now the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC), the country&rsquo;s premier public health institute. After a decisive national eradication programme, the nation became malaria-free in 1951. Brazil had also controlled a burgeoning malaria epidemic with insecticides.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Meanwhile, new weapons had emerged. The long-lasting insecticide DDT was already being widely used and killed mosquitoes easily. A new drug called chloroquine did the same to Plasmodium. Armed with these tools and buoyed by earlier successes, the World Health Organization formally launched the Global Malaria Eradication Programme in 1955. DDT was sprayed in countless homes. Chloroquine was even added to table salt in some countries. It was as ambitious a public health initiative as has ever been attempted.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w63qc\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It worked to a point. Malaria fell dramatically in Taiwan, Sri Lanka, India, the Caribbean, the Balkans, and parts of the south Pacific. But ultimately the problem was too big, the plan too ambitious. It barely made a dent in sub-Saharan Africa, where public health infrastructure was poor and malaria was most prevalent. And its twin pillars soon crumbled as &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;evolved resistance to chloroquine and mosquitoes evolved resistance to DDT. The disease bounced back across much of Asia and the western Pacific.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 1969, the eradication programme was finally abandoned. Despite several successes, its overall failure had a chilling impact on malaria research. Investments from richer (and now unaffected) countries dwindled, save for a spike of interest during the Vietnam War. The best minds in the field left for fresher challenges. Malaria, now a tropical disease of poor people, became unfashionable.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&ldquo;Rosbif and the frog&rdquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Fran&ccedil;ois Nosten always wanted to travel. His father, a sailor on merchant ships, returned home with stories of far-flung adventures and instilled a deep wanderlust. Nosten&rsquo;s original plan was to work on overseas development projects, but one of his teachers pushed him down a different path. &ldquo;He said the best thing you can do if you want to travel anywhere is to be a doctor. That&rsquo;s why I started medical school.&rdquo; As soon as he graduated, he joined M&eacute;decins Sans Fronti&egrave;res and started living the dream. He flew off to Africa and South-east Asia, before arriving in Thailand in 1983. There, he started treating refugees from Myanmar in camps along the Thai border.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 1985, an English visitor arrived at the camps and Nosten took him for a random tourist until he started asking insightful questions about malaria. That man was Nick White. A British clinician, he was drawn to Bangkok in 1980 by the allure of the tropics and a perverse desire to study something unfashionable. The University of Oxford had just set up a new tropical medicine research unit in collaboration with Bangkok&rsquo;s Mahidol University, and White was the third to join.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The rosbif and the frog&rdquo;, as Nosten puts it, bonded over an interest in malaria, a desire to knuckle down and get things done, and a similar grouchy conviviality. They formed a close friendship and started working together.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w6cjn\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 1986, they set up a field station for White&rsquo;s Bangkok research unit: little more than a centrifuge and microscope within Nosten&rsquo;s rickety house. Three years later, Nosten moved to Shoklo, the largest refugee camp along the Thai&ndash;Myanmar border and home to around 9,000 people. Most were Karen &ndash; the third largest of Myanmar&rsquo;s 130 or so ethnic groups &ndash; who were fleeing persecution from the majority Bamar government. Nosten worked out of a bamboo hospital &ndash; the first Shoklo Malaria Research Unit.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Malaria was rife. Floods were regular. Military leaders from both Thailand and Myanmar occasionally ordered Nosten to leave. Without any electricity, he often had to use a mirror to angle sunlight into his microscope. He loved it. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not a city person,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;I couldn&rsquo;t survive in Bangkok very well. I wasn&rsquo;t alone in Shoklo but it was sufficiently remote.&rdquo; The immediacy of the job and the lack of bureaucracy also appealed. He could try out new treatments and see their impact right away. He trained local people to detect &lt;em&gt;Plasmodium&lt;/em&gt; under a microscope and help with research. He even met his future wife &ndash; a Karen teacher named Colley Paw, who is now one of his right-hand researchers (White was the best man at their wedding). These were the best years of his life.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Shoklo years ended in 1995 after a splinter faction of Karen started regularly attacking the camps, in a bid to force the refugees back into Myanmar. &ldquo;They came in and started shooting,&rdquo; says Nosten. &ldquo;We once had to hide in a hole for the night, with bullets flying around.&rdquo; The Thai military, unable to defend the scattered camps, consolidated them into a single site called Mae La &ndash; a dense lattice of thatch-roofed houses built on stilts, which now contains almost 50,000 people. Nosten went with them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w6chb\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He has since expanded the Shoklo Unit into a huge hand that stretches across the region. Its palm is a central laboratory in the town of Mae Sot, where Nosten lives, and the fingers are clinics situated in border settlements, each with trained personnel and sophisticated facilities. The one in Mae La has a $250,000 neonatal care machine, and can cope with everything short of major surgery. Nosten has also set up small &lsquo;malaria posts&rsquo; along the border. These are typically just volunteer farmers with a box of diagnostic tests and medicine in their house.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know anybody else who could have done what Fran&ccedil;ois has done,&rdquo; says White. &ldquo;He&rsquo;ll underplay the difficulties but between the physical dangers, politics, logistical nightmares, and the fraught conditions of the refugees, it&rsquo;s not been easy. He&rsquo;s not a shrinking violet.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thanks to Nosten&rsquo;s network, locals know where to go if they feel unwell, and they are never far from treatments. That is vital. If infected people are treated within 48 hours of their first symptoms, their parasites die before they get a chance to enter another mosquito and the cycle of malaria breaks. &ldquo;You deploy early identification and treatment, and malaria goes away,&rdquo; says Nosten. &ldquo;Everywhere we&rsquo;ve done this, it&rsquo;s worked.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;New hope?&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; When Nosten and White teamed up in the 1980s, their first success was showing that a new drug called mefloquine was excellent at curing malaria, and at preventing it in pregnant women. Most drugs had fallen to resistant parasites and the last effective one &ndash; quinine &ndash; involved a week of nasty side-effects. Mefloquine was a godsend.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But within five years, &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;had started to resist it too. &ldquo;We tried different things like increasing the dose, but we were clearly losing the drug,&rdquo; says Nosten. &ldquo;We saw more and more treatment failures, patients coming back weeks later with the same malaria. We were really worried that we wouldn&rsquo;t have any more options.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Salvation came from China. In 1967, Chairman Mao Zedong launched a covert military initiative to discover new antimalarial drugs, partly to help his North Vietnamese allies, who were losing troops to the disease. It was called Project 523. A team of some 600 scientists scoured 200 herbs used in traditional Chinese medicine for possible antimalarial chemicals. They found a clear winner in 1971 &ndash; a common herb called qing hao (Artemisia annua or sweet wormwood). Using hints from a 2,000-year-old recipe for treating haemorrhoids, they isolated the herb&rsquo;s active ingredient, characterised it, tested it in humans and animals, and created synthetic versions. &ldquo;This was in the aftermath of the Cultural Revolution,&rdquo; says White. &ldquo;Society had been ripped apart, there was still a lot of oppression, and facilities were poor. But they did some extremely good chemistry.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The results were miraculous. The new drug annihilated even severe forms of chloroquine-resistant malaria, and did so with unparalleled speed and no side-effects. The team named it Qinghaosu. The West would know it as artemisinin. Or, at least, they would when they found out about it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w6795\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Project 523 was shrouded in secrecy, and few results were published. Qinghaosu was already being widely used in China and Vietnam when the first English description appeared in the Chinese Medical Journal in 1979. Western scientists, suspicious about Chinese journals and traditional medicine, greeted it with scepticism and wasted time trying to develop their own less effective versions. The Chinese, meanwhile, were reluctant to share their new drug with Cold War enemies.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;During this political stalemate, White saw a tattered copy of the 1979 paper. He travelled to China in 1981, and returned with a vial of the drug, which he still keeps in a drawer in his office. He and Nosten began studying it, working out the right doses, and testing the various derivatives.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;They realised that artemisinin&rsquo;s only shortcoming was a lack of stamina. People clear it so quickly from their bodies that they need seven daily doses to completely cure themselves. Few complete the full course. White&rsquo;s ingenious solution was to pair the new drug with mefloquine &ndash; a slower-acting but longer-lasting partner. Artemisinin would land a brutal shock-and-awe strike that destroyed the majority of parasites, mefloquine would mop up the survivors. If any parasites resisted the artemisinin assault, mefloquine would finish them off. &lt;em&gt;Plasmodium&lt;/em&gt; would need to resist both drugs to survive the double whammy, and White deemed that unlikely. Just three days of this artemisinin combination therapy (ACT) was enough to treat virtually every case of malaria. In theory, ACTs should have been resistance-proof.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Nosten started using them along the Thai&ndash;Myanmar border in 1994 and immediately saw results. Quinine took days to clear the parasites and left people bed-ridden for a week with dizzy spells. ACTs had them returning to work after 24 hours.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w689n\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But victories in malaria are often short-lived. In the early 2000s, the team started hearing rumours from western Cambodia that ACTs were becoming less effective. White tried to stay calm. He had heard plenty of false alarms about incurable Cambodian patients, but it always turned out that they were taking counterfeit drugs. &ldquo;I was just hoping it was another of those,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It was not. In 2006, Harald Noedl from the Medical University of Vienna started checking out the rumours for himself. In the Cambodian village of Ta Sanh, he treated 60 malaria patients with artesunate (an artemisinin derivative) and found that two of them carried exceptionally stubborn parasites. These infections cleared in four to six days, rather than the usual two. And even though the patients stayed in a clinic outside any malaria hotspots, their parasites returned a few weeks later.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I first presented those data in November 2007 and as expected, people were very sceptical,&rdquo; says Noedl. After all, a pair of patients is an epidemiological blip. Still, this was worrying enough to prompt White&rsquo;s team to run their own study in another nearby village. They got even worse news. The 40 people they treated with artesunate took an average of 3.5 days to clear their parasites, and six of them suffered from rebounding infections within a month. &ldquo;Rapid parasite clearance is the hallmark of artemisinins,&rdquo; says Arjen Dondorp, one of White&rsquo;s colleagues based in Bangkok. &ldquo;That property suddenly disappeared.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Despite the hopes that ACTs would forestall artemisinin&rsquo;s expiry, resistance had arrived, just as it had done for other antimalarials. And, as if to rub salt in the wound, it had come from the same damn place.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Hyper mutators&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; Why has a small corner of western Cambodia, no bigger than Wales or New Jersey, repeatedly given rise to drug-beating parasites?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;White thinks that the most likely explanation is the region&rsquo;s unregulated use of antimalarial drugs. China supplied artemisinin to the tyrannical Khmer Rouge in the late 1970s, giving Cambodians access to it almost two decades before White conceived of ACTs. Few used it correctly. Some got ineffective doses from counterfeit pills. Others took a couple of tablets and stopped once their fever disappeared. &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;was regularly exposed to artemisinin without being completely wiped out, and the most resistant parasites survived to spread to new hosts. There is a saying among malariologists: &ldquo;The last man standing is the most resistant.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Genetic studies hint at other explanations. Early last year, Dominic Kwiatkowski from the University of Oxford showed that some &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;strains from west Cambodia have mutations in genes that repair faults in their DNA, much like some cancer cells or antibiotic-resistant bacteria. In other words, they have mutations that make them prone to mutating. This might also explain why, in lab experiments, they develop drug resistance more quickly than strains from other parts of the world. Evolution is malaria&rsquo;s greatest weapon, and these &lsquo;hypermutators&rsquo; evolve in fifth gear.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Kwiatkowski&rsquo;s team also found that &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;is spookily diverse in west Cambodia. It is home to three artemisinin-resistant populations that are genetically distinct, despite living in the same small area. That is bizarre. Without obvious barriers between them, the strains ought to regularly mate and share their genes. Instead, they seem to shun each other&rsquo;s company. They are so inbred that they consist almost entirely of clones.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w6984\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Kwiatkowski suspects that these parasites descended from some lucky genetic lottery winners that accumulated the right sets of mutations for evading artemisinin. When they mate with other strains, their winning tickets break up and their offspring are wiped out by the drug. Only their inbred progeny, which keep the right combinations, survive and spread.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It undoubtedly helps that south-east Asia does not have much malaria. In West Africa, where transmission is high, a child might be infected with three to five &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;strains at any time, giving them many opportunities to mate and shuffle their genes. A Cambodian child, however, usually sees one strain at a time, and is a poor hook-up spot for the parasite. The region&rsquo;s infrastructure may also have helped to enforce the parasites&rsquo; isolation: local roads are poor, and people&rsquo;s movements were long constrained by the Khmer Rouge.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;West Cambodia, then, could be rife with &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;strains that are especially prone to evolving resistance, that get many opportunities to do so because antimalarial drugs are abused, and that easily hold on to their drug-beating mutations once they get them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;These are plausible ideas, but hard to verify since we still know very little about how exactly the parasites resist a drug. Earlier cases of resistance were largely due to mutations in single genes &ndash; trump cards that immediately made for invincible parasites. A small tweak in the crt gene, and &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;can suddenly pump chloroquine out of its cells. A few tweaks to dhps and dhfr, the genes targeted by sulfadoxine and pyrimethamine, and the drug can no longer stick to its targets.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w67qd\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Artemisinin seems to be a trickier enemy. Curiously, &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;takes a long time to evolve resistance to artemisinin in lab experiments, much longer than in the wild. Those strains that do tend to be weak and unstable. &ldquo;I suspect you need a complicated series of genetic changes to make a parasite that&rsquo;s not lethally unfit in the presence of these drugs,&rdquo; says White. &ldquo;It would be unusual if this was a single mutation.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Practices such as unregulated drug use and misuse may help encourage and accelerate the rate of such changes out in the field. Kwiatkowski&rsquo;s study suggests that the parasites may have evolved artemisinin resistance several times over, perhaps through a different route each time. Several groups are racing to find the responsible mutations, with news of the first few breaking in December 2013. That&rsquo;s the key to quickly identifying resistant parasites and treating patients more efficiently. (Currently, you can only tell if someone has artemisinin-resistant malaria by treating them and seeing how long they take to get better.) &ldquo;We want to be able to track resistance using blood spots on filter paper,&rdquo; says Chris Plowe at the University of Maryland School of Medicine, whose group is one of those in the race.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But time is running out. From its origins in Cambodia, resistance has reached the Thai&ndash;Myanmar border. Nosten has shown that the proportion of patients who are still infected after three days of ACT has increased from zero in 2000 to 28% in 2011. Most are still being cured, but as artemisinin becomes less effective, its partner drug will have to mop up more surviving parasites. Plasmodium will evolve resistance to the partner more quickly, driving both drugs towards uselessness.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This is already happening in western Cambodia, where ACTs are failing up to a quarter of the time and many people are still infected a month later. Long-lasting infections will provide parasites with more chances to jump into mosquitoes, and then into healthy humans. Malaria cases will rise. Deaths will follow. &ldquo;This is the silence before the storm,&rdquo; says Arjen Dondorp. &ldquo;The threat is still slightly abstract and there&rsquo;s still not that much malaria, which doesn&rsquo;t help with a sense of urgency. If we suddenly see malaria exploding, then it&rsquo;ll be a clear emergency, but it&rsquo;ll also be too late.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Radical change&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; In his office at Mahidol University, Nick White is surrounded by yellowing monographs of old malaria research and overlooked by a wall-mounted mosaic of drug packets made by his daughter. He is now the chairman of the Mahidol&ndash;Oxford Tropical Medicine Research Unit and a mentor to the dozens of researchers within. He is gently ranting.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Everything to do with change in malaria meets with huge resistance,&rdquo; he says. He means political resistance, not the drug kind. He means the decade it took for the international community to endorse ACTs despite the evidence that they worked. He means the &ldquo;treacle of bureaucracy&rdquo; that he and Nosten swim through in their push to eliminate malaria.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The global response to artemisinin resistance has been a bit pathetic. Everyone will tell you how important it is and there have been any number of bloody meetings. But there is little appetite for radical change.&rdquo; He misses the old days when &ldquo;you could drive a Land Rover across borders in your khaki shorts and spray things and do stuff&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;From the outside, things look rosier. Malaria is fashionable again, and international funding has gone up by 15 times in the last decade. Big organisations seem to be rallying behind the banner of elimination. In April, the World Health Organisation published a strategy called The Emergency Response to Artemisinin Resistance.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;It&rsquo;s a marvellous plan,&rdquo; White says drily. &ldquo;It says all the right things, but we haven&rsquo;t done anything.&rdquo; It follows two other strategies that were published in 2011 and 2012, neither of which slowed the spread of artemisinin resistance. Elimination became a dirty word after the noisy failures of the 1950s and 60s, and the new strategies look like the same old tactics for controlling malaria, presented under the guise of eradicating it. &ldquo;They&rsquo;re prescriptions for inertia,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w6bs4\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Worse, they are channelling funds into ineffective measures. Take insecticide-treated bednets, a mainstay of malaria control. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ve had meetings with WHO consultants who said, &lsquo;We don&rsquo;t want to hear a word against bednets. They always work.&rsquo; But how cost-effective are they, and what&rsquo;s the evidence they work in this region? The mosquitoes here bite early in the evening. And who&rsquo;s getting malaria? Young men. Are they all tucked up in their bednets by 6 o&rsquo;clock? No. They&rsquo;re in the fields and forests. Come on! It&rsquo;s obvious.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He says that resources could be better devoted to getting rid of fake drugs and monotherapies where artemisinin is not paired with a partner. That would preserve ACTs for as long as possible. The world also needs better surveillance for resistant parasites. White is helping with that by chairing the World-Wide Anti-Malarial Resistance Network &ndash; a global community of scientists who are rapidly collecting data on how quickly patients respond to drugs, the presence of resistance genes, the numbers of fake drugs, and more.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;White also wants to know if artemisinin-resistant parasites from south-east Asia can spread in African mosquitoes. Hundreds of mosquito species can transmit malaria, but &lt;em&gt;P. falciparum &lt;/em&gt;is picky about its hosts. If resistant strains need time to adapt to new carriers, they might be slow to spread westwards. If they can immediately jump into far-off species, they are a plane ride away from Africa. &ldquo;That changes your containment strategy,&rdquo; says White, &ldquo;but stupidly, it&rsquo;s cut out of every research application we&rsquo;ve ever made.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He is pessimistic. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m pretty confident we won&rsquo;t win but I think we should try a lot harder than we have been. If we didn&rsquo;t pull out all the stops and kids start dying of artemisinin-resistant malaria, and we can trace the genetic origins of those parasites to south-east Asia, we shouldn&rsquo;t sleep easy in our beds.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Elimination plan&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; When Nosten&rsquo;s team first arrived at Hka Naw Tah in February, they slept and worked from the village&rsquo;s unassuming temple. Using development funds from their grant, they put up a water tower and supplied electricity for the local school. In return, the villagers built them a clinic &ndash; a spacious, open-sided hut with a sloping tin roof, benches sitting on a dirt floor, a couple of tables holding boxes of drugs and diagnostic kits, treatment rooms, and a computer station. It took just two days to erect.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Karen respect strong leadership but there is an easy-going camaraderie in the clinic. When we arrive, one of the research assistants is napping across a bench. Nosten walks over and sits on him. &ldquo;You see, and I think this is a good sign, that it&rsquo;s hard to tell who&rsquo;s the boss and who&rsquo;s the patient,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Most of the villagers don&rsquo;t seem sick, but many of them have malaria nonetheless. Until recently, Nosten&rsquo;s team had always searched for the parasites by examining a drop of blood under a microscope. If someone is sick, you can see and count the Plasmodium in their red blood cells. But in 2010, they started collecting millilitres of blood &ndash; a thousand times more than the usual drops &ndash; and searching for Plasmodium&rsquo;s DNA. Suddenly, the proportion of infected people shot up from 10&ndash;20% to 60&ndash;80%. There are three, four, maybe six times as many infected people as he thought.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w6c33\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;We didn&rsquo;t believe it at first,&rdquo; says Nosten, &ldquo;but we confirmed it and re-confirmed it.&rdquo; Perhaps the tests were giving false positives, or picking up floating DNA from dead parasites? No such luck &ndash; when the team treated people with ACTs, the hidden parasites disappeared. They were real.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;These &lsquo;sub-microscopic infections&rsquo; completely change the game for elimination. Treating the sick is no longer good enough because the disease could bounce back from the hordes of symptomless carriers. The strike will have to be swift and decisive. If it&rsquo;s half-hearted, the most resistant parasites will survive and start afresh. In malarial zones, you need to treat almost everyone, clearing the parasites they didn&rsquo;t even know they had. This is Nosten&rsquo;s goal in the border villages like Hka Naw Tah. He has support from the Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation, one of the few large funders to have truly grasped the urgency of the situation and who are &ldquo;very much in the mood for elimination&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Killing the parasites is easy: it just involves three days of ACTs. Getting healthy people to turn up to a clinic and take their medicine is much harder. The team have spent months on engagement and education. The clinic is dotted with posters explaining the symptoms of malaria and the biology of mosquitoes. Earlier this morning, Honey Moon, a Karen woman who is one of Nosten&rsquo;s oldest colleagues, knocked on the doors of all the absentees from the last round to persuade them to come for tests. As a result, 16 newcomers turned up for treatments, bringing the team closer to the full 393. Nosten is pleased. &ldquo;In this village, I&rsquo;m quite optimistic that most people will be free of the parasite,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another village down the river is proving more difficult. They are more socially conservative and have a poorer understanding of healthcare. There are two factions of Karen there, one of which is refusing to take part to spite their rivals. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a good lesson for us,&rdquo; says Nosten. &ldquo;These situations will be elsewhere.&rdquo; Eliminating malaria is not just about having the right drug, the deadliest insecticide, or the most sensitive diagnostic test. It is about knowing people, from funders to villagers. &ldquo;The most important component is getting people to agree and participate,&rdquo; says Nosten. It matters that he has been working in the region for 30 years, that the Shoklo unit is a familiar and trusted name in these parts, that virtually all his team are Karen. These are the reasons that give Nosten hope, despite the lack of political will.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If the strategy looks like it is working after a year, they will start scaling up. Eventually, they hope to cover the entire sinuous border. I ask Nosten if he would ever consider leaving. He pauses. &ldquo;Even if I wanted to go somewhere else, I&rsquo;m more or less a prisoner of my own making,&rdquo; he says. He would need to find a replacement first &ndash; a leader who would command respect among both the Karen and malaria researchers, and would be willing to relocate to a place as remote as Mae Sot. It is hard to imagine a second person who would tick all those boxes. Surrounded by airborne parasites, spreading resistance, and border-hopping refugees, Fran&ccedil;ois Nosten is stuck. He would not have it any other way.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;This article was fist appeared in &lt;a href=\"http://www.mosaicscience.com/\"&gt;Mosaic&lt;/a&gt;, which &lt;/em&gt;&lt;em&gt;is dedicated to exploring the science of life,&lt;em&gt; and reproduced under a Creative Commons licence. For more Mosaic&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/em&gt; &lt;em&gt;articles &lt;a href=\"http://www.mosaicscience.com\"&gt;click here&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":null,"CollectionType":"column","Description":"Exploring the science of life. A new publication from the Wellcome Trust, Mosaics in-depth articles and films tell the stories behind biomedical research and its impact on society. Republished here under a Creative Commons licence.","Name":"Mosaic@Future","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Mosaic@Future","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:39:39.343934Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"f08d5033-0f4e-4c47-9b7e-60d1eff60ee3","Id":"wwfuture/column/mosaicfuture","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:39:39.343934Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/mosaicfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:39:39.343934Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"f08d5033-0f4e-4c47-9b7e-60d1eff60ee3","Id":"wwfuture/column/mosaicfuture","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:39:39.343934Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/mosaicfuture"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/column/mosaicfuture","_id":"559a669f82f2d2dbf7bcb939"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Health","Name":"Health","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Health"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:44:06.503434Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"00a9a33a-e393-4d09-8029-7e9d5bd1d8cb","Id":"wwfuture/section/health","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:52:37.074442Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/health"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/health","_id":"559a66a082f2d2dbf7bcb96d"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-03-25T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"How malaria defeats our drugs","HeadlineShort":"Malaria's next ticking timebomb","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Our best drug hope for tackling the global killer is becoming resistant in a small region in south-east Asia. What happens next will decide the fate of millions.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Description":"","LinkUrl":"http://www.mosaicscience.com","Name":"Mosaic","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:50Z","Entity":"partner","Guid":"714f45ba-296d-494f-a48e-c77f73e85c70","Id":"wwfuture/partner/mosaic","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:50Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"mosaic"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:partner:wwfuture/partner/mosaic","_id":"559a69b782f2d2dbf7bd396d"}],"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;In 1987, an American pharmaceutical executive called Mary Ann Leeper flew to Copenhagen to get a first-hand look at what she thought might be the world&rsquo;s next great health innovation. She didn&rsquo;t expect to find it tucked away inside an old cigar box.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When she arrived at the old farmhouse owned by Danish doctor and inventor Lasse Hessel, he opened the door with a cigar in his mouth. Then he fetched the box. &ldquo;Inside were all these bits and pieces &ndash; metal, plastic, all different kinds of stuff,&rdquo; Leeper recalls. &ldquo;I took a deep breath and thought, &lsquo;Holy mother &ndash; what have I gotten myself into?&rsquo;&rdquo; Somehow, these bits and pieces fit together to form a contraption that women could wear during sex to prevent pregnancy and sexually transmitted infections &ndash; the world&rsquo;s first female condom.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The presentation may have been unconventional, but Leeper and her colleagues at Wisconsin Pharmacal had high hopes for Hesse&rsquo;s invention. &ldquo;The Aids crisis in the United States was just fully being recognised, and it was clear to us that for women to have a product that they could use to help protect themselves would be a good thing,&rdquo; Leeper says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Indeed, when Wisconsin Pharmacal finally introduced the female condom to the US in 1993, public health experts hailed it as a game-changer. The condom, a polyurethane pouch inserted into the vagina before sex, would protect women from sexually transmitted infections even if their male partners refused to wear condoms.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Technically, the female condom works. When used correctly, it reduces a woman&rsquo;s risk of contracting HIV by around 94&ndash;97% each time she had sex, according to estimates. Studies show that making female condoms available alongside the male version increases the percentage of sexual acts that are protected, and decreases the prevalence of sexually transmitted infections.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet, two decades after its much-celebrated introduction, the female condom still isn&rsquo;t living up to its potential. Less intuitive and familiar than the male condom, the device simply never caught on. Journalists mocked it, clinicians ignored it, and women shunned it, claiming that the condom was aesthetically unappealing and technically difficult to master. Today, only 1.6% of all condoms distributed worldwide are female condoms.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There may finally be an opening to change the female condom&rsquo;s fate. For years, a handful of researchers, engineers and entrepreneurs have been quietly tinkering with the device. Their efforts are now maturing and an assortment of redesigned and reinvented female condoms are beginning to make their way onto the market. The introduction of new, more user-friendly products &ndash; coupled with renewed efforts to promote the technology around the globe &ndash; may finally be positioning the female condom for a breakthrough.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Barrier protection&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;From the start, the female condom was a difficult project &ndash; far more difficult than Leeper had bargained for. After buying the rights to the technology, Leeper and her colleagues at Wisconsin Pharmacal needed to turn Hessel&rsquo;s prototype into a marketable product. After some tweaking, they ended up with a thin polyurethane pouch with a flexible ring at each end. A woman would insert the device by squeezing the ring that sits in the closed end of the pouch and pushing it into her vagina. Once expanded inside the vagina, this inner ring would keep the condom in place. The larger ring at the open end of the pouch would sit outside the vagina, covering the external genitalia. When a man ejaculated, the internal condom pouch would trap his semen, preventing pregnancy and sexually transmitted infections.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But before Wisconsin Pharmacal could put the condom on the market, they needed approval from the US Food and Drug Administration (FDA). Because the female condom was an utterly new kind of product, the FDA decided to regulate it as a Class III medical device, a category that is generally reserved for &ldquo;high-risk&rdquo; medical equipment &ndash; such as pacemakers and certain lasers &ndash; and that requires the highest level of regulatory scrutiny. (The FDA classifies male condoms as Class II medical devices, so they are subject to fewer controls and don't require pre-market approval.)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t7yhv\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It took six years for the female condom to wind its way through the regulatory system, and when the government finally approved it, in 1993, Leeper breathed a sigh of relief. &ldquo;I thought that the hardest part was going to be getting it through FDA because it was really difficult and they kept changing the specifications, the requirements, the clinical studies,&rdquo; she says. But she was wrong. The hard part was just beginning.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As Wisconsin Pharmacal prepared to launch the Reality female condom in the US (it would go by other brand names in other countries), they made all the standard arrangements, hiring sales reps to visit medical practices and commissioning a big advertising firm to market the product directly to consumers. &ldquo;We did all the checklist things that you&rsquo;re supposed to do,&rdquo; Leeper says. &ldquo;And we fell flat on our face.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The challenge, in part, was the era, and the public&rsquo;s squeamishness about a sex-related product. &ldquo;In those days, you couldn&rsquo;t talk &lsquo;condom&rsquo; out loud,&rdquo; Leeper recalls. &ldquo;Male condoms were referred to as &lsquo;rubbers&rsquo;. You said them in a whisper and they were held behind the counter by the pharmacist.&rdquo; And although the Aids crisis was raging, for many American women, the risk of contracting HIV was abstract, something that happened to other people and not to them. While women in focus groups had said they liked the &lt;em&gt;idea&lt;/em&gt; of the condom, according to Leeper, &ldquo;when push came to shove, when they were in that bedroom, the female condom was out on the dining-room table&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There were other barriers, too. The condoms cost as much as $5 a piece, compared to male condoms, which can typically be had for $1 or less. Krissy Ferris recalls hearing about female condoms when she was a student at Oberlin College in Ohio, but the price was a deterrent. &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t actually try one until I got free samples,&rdquo; recalls Ferris, who now works at a medical practice in Cleveland, Ohio. &ldquo;Am I going to buy $6 worth of condoms to try this out a couple times? It seems like maybe not. You&rsquo;re getting male condoms for free everywhere when you&rsquo;re in your 20s.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Scream test&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What&rsquo;s more, the female condom was, frankly, strange. Unlike the male condom, which is sold rolled up and compressed, the female condom came fully open. Women and men alike were turned off by the unfamiliar, big, plastic-bag-like device they found when they undid the packet. Though some women did eventually come to like the condoms, there was a definite learning curve and as many as one-third to one-half of women had difficulty inserting them. Once in place, the condom had a tendency to squeak or rustle during sex.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The media pounced on these complaints, and utterly skewered the female condom. They ridiculed its aesthetics with seemingly limitless creativity. As sociologist Amy Kaler recounts in her 2004 paper on the condom&rsquo;s introduction, journalists compared the product to: &ldquo;a jellyfish, a windsock, a fire hose, a colostomy bag, a Baggie, gumboots, a concertina, a plastic freezer bag, something to line Boston&rsquo;s Inner Harbor with, a cross between a test tube and a rubber glove, Edvard Munch&rsquo;s The Scream, something designed for a female elephant, something out of the science-fiction cartoon The Jetsons, a raincoat for a Slinky toy, or a &lsquo;contraption used to punish fallen virgins in the Dark Ages.&rsquo;&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t8n6l\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Though the media treatment was especially harsh, journalists &ldquo;were picking up on what were genuine design issues of the first generation of the female condom,&rdquo; says Kaler, an assistant professor at the University of Alberta in Canada. &ldquo;It wasn&rsquo;t the most beautiful thing in the world. It was easy to make fun of. It was kind of laughed out of existence before it really got a chance to take off.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Still, there were signs that Wisconsin Pharmacal was onto something. In 1995, two years after bringing the condom to market, Leeper got a call from an official at Zimbabwe&rsquo;s Ministry of Health and Child Welfare. The health worker had received a petition demanding that the government of Zimbabwe bring the female condom into the country. It had been signed by 30,000 women.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Though Leeper had initially envisioned selling the female condom in America&rsquo;s private sector, the call from Zimbabwe, coupled with the condom&rsquo;s poor reception in the US, prompted the company to shift course.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;International nonprofit groups and aid organisations have long been big buyers of male condoms. The United Nations Population Fund (UNFPA), the US Agency for International Development, Population Service International and others regularly purchase male condoms in bulk and then donate them &ndash; or sell them at a deeply subsidised price &ndash; to clinics and programmes that serve particularly high-risk populations.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 1996, Wisconsin Pharmacal changed its name to the Female Health Company and began to focus on this global public sector, working with governments, global health organisations and aid agencies to get the condoms into the hands of at-risk women in low-income countries. The female condom became a particularly important tool in several countries in sub-Saharan Africa, where, in the early 2000s, 60% of new HIV diagnoses were made in women, who often contracted the virus through their long-term partners.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Sexual revolution&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Patience Kunaka, who was teaching nursing and midwifery students in Zimbabwe when she first heard about female condoms, knew these risks all too well. Two of her cousins had died of Aids-related causes, and three additional family members were infected with HIV. Like many other women, she was not initially impressed by the female condom. &ldquo;When I first saw one my immediate reaction was, &lsquo;Wow! How does it remain inside with penile movement?&rsquo;&rdquo; she recalls. &ldquo;I thought it would be sliding in and out and what a messy act! I also thought the plastic would crumble inside me causing discomfort.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t7ynk\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But Kunaka suspected her partner at the time of being unfaithful and was &ndash; in her own words &ndash; &ldquo;obsessed about sexual hygiene&rdquo;, so she decided to give the female condom a shot. It didn&rsquo;t go well at first. &ldquo;I had problems inserting it and felt discomfort from the inner ring,&rdquo; she says. Slowly, after some practice, she got the hang of it. Kunaka even came to like the device, especially &ldquo;the fact that I don&rsquo;t have to beg my partner to use a condom&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;She became a female-condom convert. &ldquo;In my African context, where men are at liberty to have as many partners as they can have, they give me power to negotiate for safer sex,&rdquo; she says. She even went on to get a job as the condoms and training manager for Population Service International Zimbabwe, and now spends her days spreading the word about female condoms to men and women throughout the country.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The female condom received a better reception in Africa than it had in the US, and as the Female Health Company sought to expand its global reach, it tweaked its original product, switching from a polyurethane condom to one made of nitrile, the same material used in many medical gloves. The nitrile condom, called the FC2, is significantly cheaper than its polyurethane predecessor, now commonly referred to as the FC1, and also less noisy during sex. In 2007, the UNFPA &lsquo;pre-qualified&rsquo; the FC2, making it eligible for bulk purchasing by public-sector agencies, and between 2007 and 2010, the number of female condoms distributed globally doubled from 25 million to 50 million.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Not bad for a contraceptive device that&rsquo;s been likened to The Scream&lt;em&gt;, &lt;/em&gt;but it&rsquo;s still a drop in the ocean compared to male condoms. For every female condom that the major donor organisations purchase, they buy 71 male condoms. And although female condoms have got cheaper, price remains a limiting factor. An aid agency purchasing the FC2 in bulk will pay anywhere from $0.55 to $0.88 per condom but can get male condoms for as little as $0.02 a piece.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In many clinics in low-income countries, the supply of female condoms can be inconsistent, and the situation&rsquo;s not much better in the private sector. While pharmacy shelves overflow with male condoms of every imaginable kind &ndash; ribbed, studded, ultrathin, warming, aloe-enriched, neon pink, glow-in-the-dark, bubblegum-flavoured &ndash; it can be difficult to find female condoms for sale at all.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In some places, the condoms are stigmatised, thanks to clinical trials and distribution programmes that initially focused on sex workers. Elsewhere, the devices are still saddled with the baggage of the product&rsquo;s first, failed introduction. In March 2013, for instance, a writer for Jezebel, a popular feminist blog and news site, published a post titled &ldquo;Stop Trying to Make Female Condoms Happen&rdquo;. She expressed scepticism that &ldquo;women will change their minds about wanting to line their vaginas like a waste paper basket&rdquo;, and concluded by noting that &ldquo;female condoms are just ew&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;More than two decades after the first female condom hit the shelves, this is not exactly the revolution that public health experts had in mind.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Painful experience&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For nearly 40 years, Path, a global health nonprofit based in Seattle, Washington, has been radically reinventing basic medical technologies. The group&rsquo;s designers and engineers, for instance, created the Uniject: a disposable syringe pre-loaded with a single dose of vaccine. They built a one-size-fits-all diaphragm, removing the need for women to visit a doctor to have one specially fitted. And they invented a portable, handheld scale that health workers can bring to home deliveries. The scale requires no electricity, can be read in the dark, and is decipherable even to birth attendants with low literacy, making it easy to identify underweight infants.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the late 1990s, Path turned its attention to the female condom. &ldquo;The Female Health Company did an incredible thing,&rdquo; says Maggie Kilbourne-Brook, a programme officer at Path. &ldquo;They created a product that had never existed, and they got it approved, and they got it registered and marketed in more than 100 countries. They actually changed people&rsquo;s perceptions of what barrier protection could be.&rdquo; But as it became apparent that the condoms weren&rsquo;t quite living up to their potential, some experts began to think that perhaps a radical makeover was in order. &ldquo;In product development,&rdquo; says Kilbourne-Brook, &ldquo;We always expect first-generation devices will need to be improved.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Path prides itself on its user-centred design process, and so, in an effort to create a female condom that women would want to use, those at Path decided to do something both radical and obvious: consult actual women. In 1998, Path began convening focus groups in four countries &ndash; South Africa, Thailand, Mexico and the US &ndash; asking women and men what they thought about female condoms and what they wanted from them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;From Durban to Seattle, it turns out that users&rsquo; desires were pretty basic: &ldquo;a product that was going to be easy to use, easy to insert, stable during use\", says Kilbourne-Brook. Plus, &ldquo;if it was possible, they wanted something that was more aesthetically pleasing&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t7ykw\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;These requests became the guiding principles for the designers and engineers working at Path&rsquo;s product development laboratory in Seattle. The team ran an iterative, multi-step design process, building prototypes of potential new condoms in the lab and then sending them out to heterosexual volunteers in each of the four countries. These women and men handled and examined each model, sharing their impressions with researchers, and couples received samples of some of the more advanced prototypes to try out in their bedrooms. The product designers used the feedback to refine &ndash; and sometimes utterly rethink &ndash; their designs and then sent new, tweaked models back for further testing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Early generations of female condoms had relied on a ring-based design. One of Path&rsquo;s first prototypes was similar, with a polyurethane pouch anchored between two fixed rings. But some women reported that it was difficult to push the inner ring into the vagina &ndash; the same complaint often made about the FC1 and FC2 &ndash; and that it was painful once inside. &ldquo;Device is stable but uncomfortable,&rdquo; one Mexican tester reported. So Path decided to scrap the rings entirely. They briefly tested a prototype that could be inserted using a tampon tube applicator, but the condom didn&rsquo;t deploy reliably.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;They spent a lot of time talking about how to improve insertion. &ldquo;We know from a user perspective, if you have a difficult time the very first time you try to use a device, a woman may never come back,&rdquo; says Kilbourne-Brook. &ldquo;We wanted this to be not only easy to use, but it needed to be easy to use for someone who&rsquo;s never used it before.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to Kilbourne-Brook, the ultimate breakthrough was inspired by feedback from testers and researchers in Thailand, who said, &ldquo;Wouldn&rsquo;t it be wonderful if you had some kind of insertion device that helped you insert it and then it got out of the way?&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Radical redesign&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;By 2003, they had hit on the solution: a dissolving applicator. The engineers created a condom that looked like a funnel, with a thin sheet of polyurethane that narrowed into a rounded tip. This tip contained the main pouch of the condom, collapsed inside a dissolving capsule. To insert the condom, women would simply push the capsule inside, much the same way they&rsquo;d insert a tampon. Once it came into contact with the moisture of the vagina, the capsule would melt away &ndash; often within 30-60 seconds &ndash; releasing the full condom pouch.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The product designers gave the condom stability by attaching four small, thin pieces of polyurethane foam to the outside of the condom. Once the pouch expanded, these foam pieces nestled up against the vaginal wall, keeping the condom in place. Like other female condoms, the model also featured a flexible outer ring to cover the external genitalia.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Between November 2003 and January 2004, 60 couples received samples of this prototype to try at home. They were impressed. Eighty-eight per cent of the women said it was easy to insert and 97% said the pouch was stable during sex. The vast majority of men and women asked said the condom was comfortable, and 98% of women and 100% of men said it allowed for satisfactory sensation during sex.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It had taken six years and more than 300 unique prototypes, but by early 2004, Path had found its female condom.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The final product, which Path named the Woman&rsquo;s Condom, is &ldquo;just a brilliant design,&rdquo; says Kaler, who was not involved in its creation. &ldquo;When you look at it visually, it isn&rsquo;t huge. It&rsquo;s clear what you do with it. And the way that it&rsquo;s been designed with these foam pads means that it doesn&rsquo;t move around.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A series of larger clinical trials &ndash; conducted in Mexico, South Africa, Thailand, China and the US &ndash; has reinforced what Path found in its initial testing, with users reporting that the Woman&rsquo;s Condom is comfortable, stable and easy to insert. Several studies have found that both men and women tend to like the Woman&rsquo;s Condom better than the FC1 and FC2. Users&rsquo; main complaint was that it does not come pre-lubricated, as the FC2 does. Instead, each condom comes with a packet of lubricant that users can apply themselves.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 2011, the Woman&rsquo;s Condom received the stamp of approval from the Shanghai Food and Drug Administration and is currently under review by the UNFPA; approval is expected in 2014. In the meantime, limited quantities are already being sold in China and South Africa.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&ldquo;Low-stress solution&rdquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Woman&rsquo;s Condom isn&rsquo;t the only new female condom on the scene. In 2012, the UNFPA pre-qualified the Cupid, which is manufactured by an Indian condom company. The Cupid relies on a ring-shaped foam sponge tucked into the closed end of the condom pouch for internal stability. Made of natural latex, the Cupid may be the cheapest female condom yet, and is now available for purchase in both the public and private sectors.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Several other condoms, each slightly different in design, are currently under UNFPA review. For instance, the Phoenurse, which is currently sold in China, comes with an optional insertion stick. Then there&rsquo;s the panty condom, in which a condom pouch is affixed to a pair of reusable panties with an opening over the vagina. Before sex, a woman can push the condom inside with her finger &ndash; or a man can with his penis &ndash; without her having to take off the undergarment.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t7zc4\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And there are still more designs in the early stages of development. Origami Condoms, based in Los Angeles, California, has developed a silicone female condom that unfolds like an accordion as it&rsquo;s pushed into the vagina. The company just completed a small phase I acceptability study &ndash; overall, participants preferred the Origami condom to the FC2, though they said the FC2 felt more stable during sex. The company plans to conduct a larger clinical trial this year.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Not every product will be right for every woman or couple, but that&rsquo;s precisely the point. &ldquo;In the studies we&rsquo;ve done, we&rsquo;ve found that some women will say, &lsquo;I really love this one and I don&rsquo;t like this one at all&rsquo;,&rdquo; says Mags Beksinska, research director at the division of maternal, adolescent and child health at the University of the Witwatersrand, South Africa. &ldquo;There are different aspects that appeal to different women in the different designs. So it would be good if there was a wider choice.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Krissy Ferris, the woman who was initially turned off by the cost of female condoms, came to see their advantages while dating a man who had trouble maintaining an erection with a male condom. &ldquo;It was definitely a barrier to male condom use, and I was not ready to compromise on using a barrier method,&rdquo; she says. The female condom was a &ldquo;low-stress&rdquo; solution.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Over the years, Ferris has tried several different products, including the FC1, the FC2 and the VA w.o.w., which, like the Cupid, uses an internal sponge to hold the condom pouch in place. Ferris found that she preferred the VA w.o.w. because the sponge made it feel more secure during sex. &ldquo;If you&rsquo;re using something with this desire for safety, having this extra measure of security was definitely a positive for me,&rdquo; she says. But the FC1 and FC2 felt more natural, she acknowledged, and for some women, that may be more important.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There is some evidence to suggest that women are more likely to have safe sex &ndash; and less likely to become pregnant or contract sexually transmitted infections &ndash; when a larger selection of contraceptive and barrier products is available.
