Giving women a greater choice in female condoms may increase the odds that they choose any female condom at all.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Meanwhile, male condoms are also getting a redesign. In November 2013, the Gates Foundation awarded 11 grants of $100,000 to designers, engineers and scientists with ideas for a &ldquo;next-generation condom&rsquo;&rdquo;&ndash; male or female &ndash; that would be easier and more pleasurable to use. The winning proposals include a male condom that is packaged with a built-in applicator, allowing the condom to be removed from its foil wrapper and donned in a single smooth motion, and a one-size-fits-all male condom designed to tighten during sex.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Practice makes perfect&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Of course, upgrading a product is merely a first step. While the FC1 certainly had its flaws, they weren&rsquo;t the only reason that female condoms didn&rsquo;t take off. &ldquo;Some technologies are harder than others,&rdquo; says Laura Frost, a partner at Global Health Insights, a research and consulting firm. &ldquo;Compared to other products where there&rsquo;s one huge issue, like affordability or awareness, this one had those barriers at every stage.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That&rsquo;s why, for the female condom to truly break through, advocates will need to invest in comprehensive marketing and education campaigns at the local, national and global levels. &ldquo;It takes more than just putting it on the shelf,&rdquo; says Susie Hoffman, an associate professor of epidemiology at Columbia University in New York.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t8mzm\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Female condoms remain less straightforward than male condoms, and one of the major lessons of the last two decades is that women often need a little bit of training to use them correctly. That means that clinicians and counsellors may have to do more than simply tell women that female condoms exist &ndash; they&rsquo;ll need to give them the opportunity to practise inserting one, either on a pelvic model or on themselves.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In addition, women may need help figuring out how to broach the subject with their male partners. Though the condoms have won praise for being female-initiated, they&rsquo;re not entirely invisible, and most men will notice if their partners are wearing them. &ldquo;In many cases, she&rsquo;s probably going to want to mention to her partner before having sex that this is a new product that she&rsquo;s going to try,&rdquo; Hoffman says. &ldquo;Ideally there&rsquo;s going to be some kind of a conversation about it, and women need help in figuring out how to do that.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Male partners are also a potential market. &ldquo;Men probably feel, when it&rsquo;s called the &lsquo;female condom&rsquo;, that it&rsquo;s not something that&rsquo;s for them,&rdquo; Mags Beksinska says. But &ldquo;there&rsquo;s no reason that a man shouldn&rsquo;t take one and bring it home and introduce it to his partner&rdquo;. In fact, she adds, once men get used to the female condom, they often prefer it to the more constricting male version. Female condoms even enhance sex for some people: the outer ring can be used to stimulate the clitoris, while the inner ring of some designs can bump up pleasurably against the tip of the penis.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The female condom may remain a tough sell, but the good news, experts say, is that there are now more organisations trying to make the pitch. &ldquo;Now we&rsquo;re seeing a much bigger coalition of advocates, which is what we need,&rdquo; Frost says. Some existing agencies, most notably the UNFPA, have stepped up their support, while champions have created a variety of new advocacy and awareness groups, including the National Female Condom Coalition in the US, and the Universal Access to Female Condoms Joint Programme, based in the Netherlands.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;New support&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Alongside this, the condom&rsquo;s supporters are getting more creative in their promotion efforts, establishing Global Female Condom Day &ndash; the first one was held on 12 September 2012 &ndash; and holding female-condom-themed fashion shows and film festivals. Several organisations have turned salons and barbershops in Zimbabwe, Malawi, Cameroon and elsewhere into female condom distribution centres, training hairdressers to promote and sell the product to both male and female clients. And all-out media blitzes in Africa &ndash; in which the condoms are promoted on billboards, television and the radio &ndash; have fed a sharp increase in demand.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I think people had kind of written off the female condom,&rdquo; says Beth Skorochod, a senior technical adviser at Population Service International. &ldquo;But now people are beginning to say, &lsquo;Ok, with more competition and more interest, maybe this deserves another look&rsquo;.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There may even be hope for the hard-to-crack private sector in higher-income countries. After winning FDA approval for the FC2 in 2009, the Female Health Company relaunched the female condom in the US, creating female condom campaigns and programmes in a handful of major American cities, including New York, San Francisco and Washington, DC.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t7zlj\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Some local US groups are also beginning to lay the groundwork for the future. Staff members at the Chicago Female Condom Campaign now show off samples of some of the newest products &ndash; including the Cupid and the Woman&rsquo;s Condom &ndash; in their training and education sessions. The goal is to make sure that healthcare providers and consumers will be familiar with the products if and when they appear in the US. But there&rsquo;s an added benefit. &ldquo;Frankly what this also does is it helps to cultivate new female condom advocates,&rdquo; says Jessica Terlikowski, who coordinates the Chicago Female Condom Campaign and the National Female Condom Coalition. Seeing other products, she says, can prompt women to ask, &ldquo;&lsquo;How can I get that?&rsquo; &lsquo;Why don&rsquo;t we have that here?&rsquo; People can&rsquo;t ask for or demand what they don&rsquo;t know about.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Women may soon have choices beyond the conventional condom. Scientists have been developing interventions that would be truly invisible to women&rsquo;s partners: oral antiretroviral pills and vaginal gels that prevent HIV. Despite the enormous excitement surrounding these drugs, they won&rsquo;t be magic bullets either, and the public-health community will still have to grapple with the thorny issues of education, access and adherence. Earlier this year, for instance, researchers announced that a clinical trial of two different HIV prevention pills and one vaginal gel, conducted among women in three African nations, failed because women weren&rsquo;t using the medications regularly.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Such outcomes are making it increasingly obvious that the global fight against HIV and other sexually transmitted infections is unlikely to be won with any one technology, no matter how elegantly designed. Instead, it will require an arsenal of weapons, a diverse array of tools that allow women and men to protect themselves. The female condom may never be as cheap or as popular as the male condom, but that doesn&rsquo;t mean it has no role to play.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Among those optimistic about the female condom&rsquo;s future is Lasse Hessel, the Danish doctor who started it all. The condom&rsquo;s champions made some mistakes in the early years, Hessel says, but he&rsquo;s encouraged by the recent resurgence of interest and the new products that are hitting the shelves. In fact, he wishes other inventors had redesigned his condom sooner, especially because there was so much room for improvement: &ldquo;How can my ugly, clumsy female condom get any worse?&rdquo; Hessel says. &ldquo;It can only get much better.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;This article was originally commissioned by &lt;a href=\"http://www.mosaicscience.com/\"&gt;Mosaic&lt;/a&gt;, and reproduced under a Creative Commons licence. For more &lt;/em&gt;&lt;em&gt;Mosaic&lt;/em&gt; &lt;em&gt;articles &lt;a href=\"http://www.mosaicscience.com\"&gt;click here&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-03-04T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"The future of safe sex?","HeadlineShort":"The future of safe sex?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Once mocked as having the erotic appeal of a jellyfish, the female condom is being reinvented as the next big thing in protective sex. Emily Anthes takes an in-depth investigation to see what chance it has of catching on this time around.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":[],"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Once mocked as having the erotic appeal of a jellyfish, the female condom is being reinvented as the next big thing in protective sex. Will it ever catch on?","SummaryShort":"Can the female condom ever be popular? ","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:17Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"8030cee6-74b6-42e0-afd4-f207000deb81","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140304-the-future-of-safe-sex","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:17Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140304-the-future-of-safe-sex"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140304-the-future-of-safe-sex","_id":"559a6a4282f2d2dbf7bd4daf"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;For the first 12 days, you go about your life normally, unaware that these may very well be your last.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Then at around day 13, the virus erupts out of infected cells and pours into your blood stream. Fever, headache, vomiting and intense body aches leave you too sick to function. At this point, however, there&rsquo;s still hope: perhaps you&rsquo;ve just contracted the flu.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Around day 17, however, those prayers are dashed. The first telltale spots begin to break out on your tongue and inside your mouth. These quickly spread across your body, concentrating in the extremities. The pea-sized bumps soon fill with a thick liquid &ndash; the detritus of your ravaged tissues &ndash; and develop a donut-like indentation. For those who dare touch them, they feel like small pebbles embedded under the skin. If you survive &ndash; and 30% of people do not &ndash; the papules will form a crust, eventually falling off as scabs and leaving you with life-long pitted scars.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That&rsquo;s what happens when you are infected by Variola major virus &ndash; better known as &lt;a href=\"http://www.bt.cdc.gov/agent/smallpox/overview/disease-facts.asp\"&gt;smallpox&lt;/a&gt;. An estimated 300 million people died from smallpox in the 20th Century alone, making it one of history&rsquo;s biggest ever viral killers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thankfully, however, smallpox was stamped out more than three decades ago, and now there are only a few samples of smallpox left on Earth. Where are they? Why are they there? And could 2014 be the year that we finally destroy the last of this killer virus once and for all?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 1975, the last naturally occurring case of Variola major turned up in a two-year old girl in Bangladesh. Finally, after three decades of eradication efforts, on 8 May&nbsp; 1980, the World Health Organization pronounced success, writing: &ldquo;The world and its peoples have won freedom from smallpox, which was a most devastating disease sweeping in epidemic form through many countries since earliest time, leaving death, blindness and disfigurement in its wake.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This was possible because unlike most deadly viruses &ndash; such as Ebola, yellow fever, HIV, or influenza &ndash; smallpox has no animal reservoir. Without human incubators, the virus cannot naturally exist.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It also doesn&rsquo;t stay alive on its own for long. In the past, some researchers and &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencemag.org/content/295/5562/2002\"&gt;news outlets&lt;/a&gt; speculated that smallpox in the frozen graves of former victims might remain in suspended animation, ready to begin a new cycle of infection should those bodies ever be dug up and thawed. Scientists have attempted to excavate corpses in frozen graves in Alaska and Siberia that contain the remains of smallpox victims, however none of the bodies contained viable viruses. &ldquo;No one feels there&rsquo;s a serious chance that global warming will melt the permafrost and unleash an epidemic,&rdquo; says Michael Lane, who served as director of the Centers for Disease Control&rsquo;s (CDC) smallpox eradication programme from 1970 until 1981, when it was declared a success and shut down.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Chilling case&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;After smallpox was eradicated from the environment, a handful of labs across the world kept live samples of the disease for research purposes, such as developing treatments and vaccines. In 1978, however, the dangers became clear. Janet Parker, a photographer working at the University of Birmingham Medical School, began complaining about headache and muscle pains. Days later, red spots appeared on her body, which medical workers wrote off as a harmless rash. Two weeks after her symptoms began, however, doctors correctly diagnosed smallpox. She was quarantined and treated for her illness, but died around two weeks later. Her mother also fell ill but survived, while her father suffered a fatal heart attack during a visit to see his daughter in the hospital.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Parker had somehow been exposed to the virus, which had likely traveled through air ducts to her office from a lab downstairs where researchers kept samples of smallpox. Henry Bedson, the head of the university&rsquo;s medical microbiology department, blamed himself for Parker&rsquo;s death and &lt;a href=\"http://news.google.com/newspapers?nid=2507&amp;dat=19780909&amp;id=PwI-AAAAIBAJ&amp;sjid=cUkMAAAAIBAJ&amp;pg=5226,1912288\"&gt;cut his throat&lt;/a&gt; several days before Parker passed away.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This distressing series of events triggered a complete rethink about keeping stores of the smallpox virus for research. &ldquo;Although we all had in the back of our minds that we needed to extirpate the virus &ndash; not just the disease &ndash; that incident made us realise that the repository of smallpox in test tubes and screw-top vials in various labs was a major problem,&rdquo; Lane says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The World Health Organization quietly put out a call for all samples of the virus to be either destroyed or sent to one of two official repositories: a CDC lab near Atlanta and the Vector Institute in Koltsovo, Siberia. A handful of samples quickly came in, arriving from India, Japan, the UK and other locations. &ldquo;It very quickly became obvious that nobody wanted the virus in their lab anyway,&rdquo; Lane says. &ldquo;They were delighted to give this stuff up.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Viral tomb&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; Currently, smallpox is known to be kept only at those two locations. (While previously unknown vials were &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/news/world-us-canada-28221185\"&gt;discovered in a Washington lab in July 2014&lt;/a&gt;, they were soon transported to Atlanta.) Both house the virus in biosafety level four (BSL-4) facilities. This level of safety &ndash; the highest possible &ndash; requires researchers to wear a positive pressure suit that resembles those worn in space and includes a tethered air supply. All manipulation with the virus, which is stored in liquid nitrogen tanks, is done in bio-safety cabinets. When exiting the lab, researchers spend seven minutes in a chemical shower to disinfect themselves, followed by a water rinse. No air escapes from within the BSL-4&rsquo;s rooms, and each year the CDC lab is shut down for a month to undergo preventative maintenance.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While the CDC is located on the fringes of bustling Atlanta, the Russian smallpox repository, Vector, has an additional safety measure &ndash; it is in the middle of nowhere.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Security at both locations is formidable. &ldquo;I was director of smallpox programmes for many years, and I live one mile from the CDC,&rdquo; Lane says. &ldquo;I can&rsquo;t even get into the building &ndash; not just the lab &ndash; where smallpox virus is kept.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Only a small number of researchers are permitted to enter these chambers. For some, it&rsquo;s just another day on the job. &ldquo;I cut my teeth working on BSL-4 viruses,&rdquo; says Peter Jahrling, director of the National Institute of Allergy and Infectious Disease&rsquo;s Integrated Research Facility, and chief of the Emerging Viral Pathogens Section. &ldquo;I work with Ebola, Lassa and Marburg, so smallpox is a walk in the park.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Others, however, maintain a sense of reverence for the disease. &ldquo;This is a virus that wreaked terrible havoc on the human population, and efforts to control and eradicate that disease were heroic,&rdquo; says Inger Damon, chief of the poxvirus and rabies branch at the CDC&rsquo;s Division of High-Consequence Pathogens and Pathology. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t think you ever become immune to that sense of awe.&rdquo;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Nowhere else?&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; So how do we know that additional smallpox samples are not also hoarded away in some terrorist hideout, or stuck in an old vial in the back of a forgotten freezer? &ldquo;We don&rsquo;t,&rdquo; Lane says. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s no way to prove a negative.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Still, things look good for the disease&rsquo;s confinement to the CDC and Vector. Since the late 1970s, as far as experts know, no one has contracted smallpox. At the same time, no terrorists have come forward claiming they have the virus and no rumours have circulated of it being kept in some secret lab.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Except, that is, for one suspicious case. In 1992, Ken Alibek &ndash; formerly Colonel Kanatzhan Alibekov &ndash; defected from Russia to the US. Alibek served as the first deputy director of Biopreparat, the Soviet Union&rsquo;s biological warfare agency, founded in 1973. After arriving in the US, Alibek began &lt;a href=\"http://www.nlm.nih.gov/nichsr/esmallpox/biohazard_alibek.pdf\"&gt;making allegations&lt;/a&gt; against his former nation, claiming that the Soviet Union had produced 50 tons of smallpox. Biopreparat, he claimed, had employed a scientific army of 30,000 people who worked on smallpox and other pathogenic weapons, such as Ebola, anthrax and plague.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Could this be true? &ldquo;It&rsquo;s no secret that smallpox virus was developed as a biological weapon and produced on an industrial scale,&rdquo; Jahrling says. &ldquo;Is it possible that someone kept a little retirement package in his freezer? It&rsquo;s not impossible.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But some &ndash; including Lane &ndash; doubt Alibek&rsquo;s claims, and point out there&rsquo;s no evidence. &ldquo;The fact that nobody knows for sure whether this happened is the major factor that makes people nervous, even though there&rsquo;s no solid reason to believe it,&rdquo; says Lane.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Last to go&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now the last remaining virus samples in Atlanta and Russia might finally be headed for eradication.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This summer, the World Health Assembly &ndash; which acts as a sort of congress for the WHO &ndash; will vote about whether or not to destroy the remaining smallpox samples. The Russian delegation has consistently voted against this path, so there is a chance that Russia would not comply if the vote turned in favour of destroying the virus. For the US, however, &ldquo;it would be extremely embarrassing for us not to go along with the official WHO recommendation,&rdquo; Lane says. &ldquo;We&rsquo;d basically be thumbing our nose at the UN.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Russians are not alone in wanting to keep smallpox. Some scientists say that more research needs to be done on the complex genetics of the virus, which could help devise more effective antivirals, for example. &ldquo;I know many in this country feel there&rsquo;s important research to be done, and that it really doesn&rsquo;t make a lot of sense to acquiesce to some symbolic act,&rdquo; Jahrling says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Damon also sees the logic in keeping smallpox around. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve changed my mind many times, and when I first came to this position at CDC, I didn&rsquo;t really see a need to use the live virus,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;But I think if we&rsquo;re going to develop a better understanding of the virus, having access to it is critical.&rdquo;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Dissenters, on the other hand, claim that most of that work can be done with the much less virulent Vaccinia virus or other related viruses. &ldquo;It almost becomes a theological argument: either you believe you need it or you don&rsquo;t,&rdquo; Jahrling says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Wouldn&rsquo;t destroying it make certain once and for all that it&rsquo;d never fall into the wrong hands though? The truth is, even it were destroyed, existing lab techniques could in principle recreate it based on the smallpox genome, which has been sequenced.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That begs the question: why hasn&rsquo;t anyone done that already? If a terrorist group, say, did have the means of creating smallpox, the effort may not be worth it compared with other methods of terrorism, such as the use of chemical weapons, says Lane. Vaccines exist to contain smallpox&rsquo;s spread, and it isn&rsquo;t the most contagious of pathogens. A smallpox outbreak would no doubt be a catastrophe, but there are worse things that could be unleashed on a population. We beat smallpox once and we could beat it again.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For now, at least, smallpox lives on &ndash; somewhere deep inside two very special labs; the only two places left on Earth where one of history&rsquo;s biggest killers still thrives.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-01-31T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Smallpox: Last refuge of an ultimate killer","HeadlineShort":"Last refuge of an ultimate killer","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"In July 2014, long forgotten vials of smallpox left in a cardboard box were discovered at a research centre near Washington. Until then, it was believed that only two places on Earth guard the last remaining samples of this killer virus. Two places on Earth guard the last vials of smallpox, but Rachel Nuwer finds that they may not be there for long","SummaryShort":"Why we havent fully eradicated smallpox","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:56Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"b80d587a-d796-4fb3-aca2-85c3bff360a1","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140130-last-refuge-of-an-ultimate-killer","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:56Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140130-last-refuge-of-an-ultimate-killer"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140130-last-refuge-of-an-ultimate-killer","_id":"559a6a3f82f2d2dbf7bd4d3f"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"ib2video","AssetVideoIb2":[],"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":null,"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Thirty years after the disease became a health crisis, the prospect of a generation living without HIV/Aids is becoming more likely. More&nbsp;effective anti-retroviral drugs and regimens are dramatically cutting the chances of an infected mother passing on the disease to her baby during pregnancy or breastfeeding.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Husband and wife team&nbsp;Annie Lennox and Dr Mitch Besser are working to create a world where&nbsp;babies are not infected with HIV and where mothers with HIV/Aids live long and healthy lives.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Mitch Besser&nbsp;is founder and medical director of&nbsp;&lt;a href=\"http://www.m2m.org/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;mothers2mothers&lt;/a&gt;, which provides education and support services to improve the prevention of mother-to-child transmission of HIV (PMTCT) in sub-Saharan Africa. It employs and trains mothers living with HIV to work in health centres &ndash; which also helps ensure they keep taking their medication. Working with some 400 health centres, it is part of the UN&rsquo;s Global Plan to end pediatric Aids by 2015.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;His wife, the former Eurythmics singer Annie Lennox, founded the&nbsp;&lt;a href=\"http://www.annielennoxsing.com/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;SING&lt;/a&gt;&nbsp;Campaign in 2007 to help prevent the spread of HIV in South Africa. Lennox is an ambassador for Oxfam, Nelson Mandela&rsquo;s 46664 Campaign, Amnesty International, and several other organizations.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Lennox and Besser spoke to BBC Future at the&nbsp;&lt;a href=\"http://www.aspenideas.org/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Aspen Ideas Festival&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;em&gt;(All background footage courtesy of Nick Fletcher/La Lennoxa Ltd)&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this video or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-09-19T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Stopping HIV transmission from mothers to children","HeadlineShort":"How to stop the spread of HIV","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Preventing the transmission of HIV from mother to child could be the first step to an Aids-free generation. Singer Annie Lennox and husband Mitch Besser are leading the fight to stop the disease being passed on.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"video","SummaryLong":"Preventing the transmission of HIV from mother to child could be the first step to an Aids-free generation, say singer Annie Lennox and husband Mitch Besser.","SummaryShort":"Annie Lennoxs battle to halt the disease  ","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:32Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"7b74c9d1-bdfb-4206-b4c2-705fc767ddae","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130919-stopping-mothers-passing-on-hiv","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:32Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130919-stopping-mothers-passing-on-hiv"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130919-stopping-mothers-passing-on-hiv","_id":"559a6a3282f2d2dbf7bd4b81"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Zack Oloo and Sam Agutu have been friends since they met at school 43 years ago. They followed similar career paths, working their way up Kenya&rsquo;s health insurance industry until each ran successful brokerage businesses in the sector. Then, a few years ago, they both quit.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;With less than 10% of Kenyans covered by health insurance, whether by private companies or the mandatory national hospital care scheme for those in formal employment, most of the population potentially faces devastating out-of-pocket expenses if they fall seriously ill.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Oloo and Agutu decided there must be a better way. So in 2008 they launched Changamka Microhealth, a company that helps Kenya&rsquo;s urban poor access and finance healthcare through use of mobile phones, mobile money and smart cards.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While all employers are required by law to enroll their employees into the National Hospital Insurance Fund, membership is voluntary for the 77% of the working population who are self-employed or work in the informal sector. The scheme covers limited hospital care solely, and as of last year, only 800,000 people from the informal sector were enrolled. Other private insurance schemes cost upwards of 50-80,000 KES ($600-900) to cover a family. The average per capita income in Kenya is only $1,800 a year.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;For self-employed Kenyans [and those in the informal economy]&hellip; formal insurance mechanisms do not exist,&rdquo; says Oloo. And they are the ones who need it the most. Even malaria treatment, which costs as little as half a US dollar can cause financial difficulties for someone making just $2-$3 a day.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Dr. G. Mbugua, who has run a small clinic called Uhuru Prestige in a low-income area of the capital Nairobi for the past seven years, says that most of his patients are uninsured.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;In Kenya, we have a problem with saving for health care,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;Most of my patients pay out of pocket. They prefer to tackle health care when someone is sick. They&rsquo;d rather buy food than save for health.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Christina Synowiec, of the Center for Health Market Innovations in Washington, DC, agrees. &ldquo;There is lack of sensitisation to insurance, and, as part of that, pre-payment is a hard concept,&rdquo; says Synowiec. &ldquo;The notion of paying in advance for access to health services is foreign, but can help in preventing financial catastrophes.&rdquo;&nbsp;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Synowiec adds that it can be difficult to identify uninsured individuals and their families, encourage enrollment and collect premiums.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet while a culture of saving among the poor is lacking because of low wages, fluid employment, and extended family emergencies, &ldquo;saving is better understood than insurance,&rdquo; Oloo says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So in 2009 Changamka launched a &ldquo;smart card&rdquo; that enabled Kenyans to save small amounts over time to cover the costs of outpatient services, drug prescriptions and consultations. Customers could buy Medi-Save cards in shops, and then add money whenever they liked using M-Pesa, the mobile money service that allows the nearly 75% of adult Kenyans who subscribe to it to use their phones to store money and make payments. Medi-Save users could then transfer extra money into an account linked to their card number simply by sending a text message.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When they attended clinics, hospitals and other healthcare providers, their cards were swiped and their accounts charged at point-of-sale terminals. Changamka pre-contracts prices with medical providers, and takes a 10% cut for services charged. Providers are happy to pay, since the system eliminates time consuming and expensive paperwork.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Life saver&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Last month, the company introduced new E-cards, which work the same way as the previous system except now providers bill patients via a secure website, meaning they don't need separate point-of-sale devices.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Before the introduction of these cards, says Oloo, many women had no way to securely save for maternity care. &ldquo;They would put their money under their pillow, but something would go wrong after a few months. For example a boyfriend would come and say there&rsquo;s an emergency and give me that money.&rdquo; To help women in this situation, the company launched a separate maternity card, to enable women to save for ante-natal, maternity and post-natal services at set prices. They also have a card for family health care.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hellen Osteno, of Komarock, a low-income area on the edge of Nairobi, bought a family card in April. &ldquo;I chose to get the card because many times I could not save,&rdquo; she said.&nbsp;&ldquo;It&rsquo;s helped me because now I don&rsquo;t have to go to the bank to deposit money or check my balance. I can check it through my handset, even in the night, if I have an emergency.&rdquo; She saves &ldquo;around 100 or 200 KES&rdquo; ($1-2) daily when she can, she says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;With the new E-card system claims are submitted online, meaning they can be traced and processed more quickly. Changamka is also now able to start digitising the medical records of its clients, as diagnosis and treatment notes are also recorded.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In future doctors and other medical professionals could gain access to patients&rsquo; digital medical histories thanks to M-Kadi and other systems like it, although just as similar schemes have provoked debate in some western countries, some Kenyans have expressed fears about privacy and security.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To date, the company has sold 12,000 family health plans, 3,000 maternity plans.&nbsp;However sometimes saving is not enough. &ldquo;We found catastrophes could not always depend on savings,&rdquo; says Agutu, &ldquo;so we designed an insurance product that would service the lower end of the market.&rdquo;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Changamka, in collaboration with one of Kenya&rsquo;s largest insurance companies British American Insurance and telecom provider Safaricom, launched a micro-insurance scheme called Lindi Jamii in November last year. Customers can simply dial *525# on a Safaricom line to sign up. A community health worker then visits to obtain a photo and take down other details such as a national ID number and family information.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Similar to their other services, customers save money via M-Pesa transfers. Once their account hits 12,000 KES, ($140), they are immediately enrolled in a family insurance scheme, covering two parents and unlimited children for 180 days.&nbsp;Besides covering typical medical expenses, the insurance also covers add on services such as income replacement during hospitalisation, funeral assistance, and dental coverage.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Changamka is doing its best to find new ways to extend coverage and facilitate payments for Kenya&rsquo;s uninsured, however it is still a long way from profiting from these schemes. Their Linda Jamii micro-insurance only has around 8,000 subscribers so far, and is currently still reliant on funding from NGOs and donor agencies. The company has shown it can use novel technologies to help insure the poor, but to be truly successful, and profitable, it will have to find a way to reach hundreds of thousands, if not millions, of uninsured Kenyans.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:16:19Z","HeadlineLong":"Smart cards that top-up health ","HeadlineShort":"Smart cards that top-up health ","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Most of Kenyas population cant afford the crippling expenses if they fall seriously ill. We should use the resources, technology and the science we have to make sure that hungry citizens have adequate food.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The idea that we don&rsquo;t do this, because we are politically motivated, or because of policy or logistical limitations to get food from point A to point B, is extremely frustrating as this is a solvable problem.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The way d&rsquo;Amato says we can feed the world is by using models that can work in communities &ndash; in one community it might be the water supply to teach farmers how to grow food, in another it might be urban gardening. Whatever it is the community has to be able to sustain it: the political will or the donations come and go, but the hungry child doesn&rsquo;t come and go.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We are able to use data in way we haven&rsquo;t been able to before. We can use software platforms and geo-mapping to measure impact. Thirty years ago, we couldn&rsquo;t do this: we knew we were feeding one hundred people, but we didn&rsquo;t know if we were feeding the right people, or whether to feed them more. Technology allows people to measure where that food went and who ate it, and therefore the impact is even more profound.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Feeding people in need allows them to focus their minds on other matters, as their basic needs are taken care of. They can contribute to their communities in ways we haven&rsquo;t thought of, because they have been distracted or having to find their food. We are beyond that, says d&rsquo;Amato, we have the ability to feed the world.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this video or anything else you have seen on Future, head desert","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1t/23/p01t23kc.jpg","Title":"atacama_desert2_crop.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01t23kc","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01t23kc","_id":"559a689b82f2d2dbf7bd1010"},{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":112366,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":549,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/1t/23/p01t23bt.jpg","SourceWidth":976,"SynopsisLong":"A
