have been looking for evidence of life in Lake Vostok, deep beneath the ice of Antarctica. (Wikimedia Commons)","SynopsisMedium":"Researchers have been looking for evidence of life in Lake Vostok, deep beneath the ice of Antarctica. (Wikimedia Commons)","SynopsisShort":"Lake Vostok","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1t/23/p01t23nt.jpg","Title":"lake_vostok_crop.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01t23nt","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01t23nt","_id":"559a689b82f2d2dbf7bd1011"}],"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Description":"&lt;p&gt;Rachel Nuwer is a science journalist who contributes to\nvenues such as The New York Times, Scientific American and Smithsonian. Her\nwebsite is &lt;a href=\"http://rachelnuwer.com/\"&gt;rachelnuwer.com&lt;/a&gt; and you can\nfollow her on twitter at &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/rachelnuwer\"&gt;@rachelnuwer&lt;/a&gt;.\nShe lives in Brooklyn.&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Rachel Nuwer","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"c575dd82-8272-4f0b-9c80-40e01bb0a58e","Id":"wwfuture/author/rachel-nuwer","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"rachel-nuwer"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/rachel-nuwer","_id":"559a668e82f2d2dbf7bcb641"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;In the Atacama Desert in northern Chile, it looks as if nothing could ever survive. It is one of the driest places in the world, and some sections of the Mars-like expanse can go 50 years without feeling a drop of rain. As poet Alonso de Ercilla put it in 1569: &ldquo;Towards Atacama, near the deserted coast, you see a land without men, where there is not a bird, not a beast, nor a tree, nor any vegetation.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet Atacama is not devoid of life. Microorganisms called endoliths have found a way to cling on, by hiding themselves inside the pores of rocks, where there&rsquo;s just enough water to survive. &ldquo;They support a whole community of organisms that eat the byproducts of their metabolism,&rdquo; says Jocelyne DiRuggiero, a microbiologist at Johns Hopkins University. &ldquo;And they&rsquo;re all just sitting right there in the rocks &ndash; it&rsquo;s quite fascinating.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Life, it seems, has an incredible knack for finding ways to persist. Indeed, microorganisms have been around for nearly four billion years, giving them ample time to adapt to some of the most extreme conditions in the natural world. But are there places left on Earth so harsh that they are rendered sterile?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t23kc\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Heat is a good starting point for answering this question. The record for heat tolerance is currently held by a group of organisms called hyperthermophile methanogens, which thrive around the edges of hydrothermal vents in the deep sea. Some of these organisms can grow at &lt;a href=\"http://www.pnas.org/content/105/31/10949.full.pdf\"&gt;temperatures of up to 122C&lt;/a&gt; (252F).&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Most researchers believe that around 150C (302F) is the theoretical cut-off point for life, however. At that temperature proteins fall apart and chemical reactions cannot occur &ndash; a quirk of the biochemistry that life on Earth (so far as we know) abides by. This means that microorganisms can thrive around hydrothermal vents, but not directly within them, where temperatures can reach up to 464C (867F). The same is true for the interior of an active volcano on land. &ldquo;I really think temperature is the most hostile parameter,&rdquo; says Helena Santos, a microbial physiologist at the New University of Lisbon and president of the &lt;a href=\"http://extremophiles.org/\"&gt;International Society for Extremophiles&lt;/a&gt;. When things get hot enough, she says, &ldquo;It&rsquo;s impossible &ndash; everything is destroyed.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t23bt\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;High pressure, by contrast, appears to be less of a problem for life. This means that heat rather than depth probably limits how far below the surface of the Earth life occurs. The centre of the Earth&rsquo;s 6,000C (10,800F) temperature certainly precludes all life, although the depth at which the cut-off occurs is still under investigation. One microorganism called &lt;em&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencemag.org/content/322/5899/275\"&gt;Desulforudis audaxviator&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/em&gt; was discovered nearly two miles (3.2km) below the Earth&rsquo;s surface, in a South African gold mine. It has not been in contact with the surface for potentially millions of years, and survives by siphoning nutrients from rocks undergoing radioactive decay.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Life exists at the other extreme, too, in subfreezing conditions. Bacteria in the genus &lt;em&gt;Psychrobacter &lt;/em&gt;can happily live below -10C (14F) in Siberian permafrost and Antarctic glacier mud. &lt;a href=\"http://www.nytimes.com/2013/02/07/science/living-bacteria-found-deep-under-antarctic-ice-scientists-say.html?_r=0\"&gt;Living cells recently turned up&lt;/a&gt; in a subglacial lake below the Antarctic ice. And Antarctica&rsquo;s hypersaline Deep Lake hosts unique &lt;a href=\"http://www.pnas.org/content/early/2013/09/25/1307090110.abstract\"&gt;salt-loving species&lt;/a&gt;, even at -20C (-4F). To survive in these environments, microrganisms possess features such as specially adapted membranes and protein structures, and anti-freeze molecules within their cells. Given that the Earth has been covered in ice multiple times since life first evolved, &ldquo;an ice-covered lake in Antarctica does not seem all that extreme,&rdquo; says Jill Mikucki, a microbiologist at the University of Tennessee.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t23nt\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Radiation typically does not deter microorganisms, either. So long as they are not in the direct pathway of an atomic blast &ndash; which would likely burn them up &ndash; they can thrive in containers of radioactive waste or near the epicentre of the Chernobyl disaster, for example. &lt;em&gt;Deinococcus radiodurans&lt;/em&gt;, one of the hardiest of the radio-resistant microorganisms, has &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0378109702009539\"&gt;survived trips into space&lt;/a&gt; and can endure radiation doses of up to 15,000 gray (the standard measure of the absorbed dose of radiation). For humans, just 5 gray causes death.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Likewise, what we register as deadly chemical environments, some extremophiles call home sweet home. Various organisms depend on arsenic, mercury or other heavy metals for their growth and survival, while others prefer cyanide. In &lt;a href=\"http://www.exploratorium.edu/kamchatka/\"&gt;the hot springs of Kamchatka in Russia&lt;/a&gt;, menageries of microorganisms metabolise using sulphur or carbon monoxide. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s hard to find a chemical that can kill all life,&rdquo; says Frank Robb, a microbiologist at the University of Maryland.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There might be isolated exceptions, however. Don Juan Pond in Antarctica is the saltiest known body of water on the planet, with salinity levels topping 40%. (The Dead Sea is around 33%). Researchers have recovered traces of microbial life from the pond, but they are still trying to determine whether it is actively growing and replicating there, or just blowing in from other locations. Don Juan counts as &ldquo;an example of a place on the surface of Earth where we might expect life, but cannot verify the presence of active life,&rdquo; says Corien Bakermans, a microbiologist at Penn State University.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For now, extreme heat and some synthetic laboratory environments might be the only sterile conditions on the planet&rsquo;s surface that it&rsquo;s possible to find zero traces of life. New organisms are regularly discovered that push the boundaries of life as we understand it, although where that line will ultimately be drawn remains unknown. As Santos says, &ldquo;What does not exist is more difficult to prove than what does exist.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even if there are some lone holdouts of sterility in the natural world, however, the environment exists in a constant state of flux &ndash; and if extremophiles teach us anything, it is that organisms are always capable of adapting. &ldquo;Give them enough time and they will find a way,&rdquo; says DiRuggiero.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":null,"CollectionType":"column","Description":"A grand tour of our planets last outposts. In a rapidly changing world, writer Rachel Nuwer sets out to find the last remaining places, people, technologies and resources of their kind.","Name":"Last Place on Earth","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Last Place on Earth","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:18:34.168332Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"98c431f6-a141-4d77-883c-be660f7c0c98","Id":"wwfuture/column/last-place-on-earth","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:18:34.168332Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/last-place-on-earth"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:18:34.168332Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"98c431f6-a141-4d77-883c-be660f7c0c98","Id":"wwfuture/column/last-place-on-earth","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:18:34.168332Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/last-place-on-earth"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/column/last-place-on-earth","_id":"559a669e82f2d2dbf7bcb936"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Science &amp; Environment","Name":"Science &amp; Environment","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Science &amp; Environment"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:51:45.984509Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"1aa4ccec-87b5-44b8-b5af-c553debaf6f6","Id":"wwfuture/section/science-environment","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:51:20.083392Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/science-environment"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/science-environment","_id":"559a66a082f2d2dbf7bcb96e"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Health","Name":"Health","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Health"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:44:06.503434Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"00a9a33a-e393-4d09-8029-7e9d5bd1d8cb","Id":"wwfuture/section/health","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:52:37.074442Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/health"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/health","_id":"559a66a082f2d2dbf7bcb96d"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-03-03T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"The last place on Earth without life","HeadlineShort":"Last place on Earth without life","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Weird and wonderful creatures can thrive in the most hostile parts on the planet, but there are a few places too harsh for even the hardiest, discovers Rachel Nuwer. ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;It can be easy to forget what life was like before the internet. For many, not a day goes by without checking email, browsing online or consulting Google. Some 1.3 billion people alive today are young enough never to have experienced anything else. Yet has the network of networks underpinning all this activity actually reached every part of the globe?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Various reasons still stop people accessing the internet where they live, of course. There&rsquo;s censorship, for starters. &ldquo;We don&rsquo;t get much traffic from North Korea,&rdquo; says John Graham-Cumming of &lt;a href=\"http://www.cloudflare.com/\"&gt;CloudFlare&lt;/a&gt;, a content delivery network &ndash; the equivalent of a regional parcel distribution centre, but for web traffic. &ldquo;Likewise, early in the Syrian civil war they cut off internet access and we saw a drop in traffic coming from those Syrian connections.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s also a well-established problem that many of the world&rsquo;s poorest people do not have the means or technology to log on, with just &lt;a href=\"http://www.itu.int/net/pressoffice/press_releases/2013/05.aspx#.Uv0POEKwKm0\"&gt;31% of people&lt;/a&gt; in the developing world using the internet, compared to 77% in the developed countries.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01s8j01\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, these political and social barriers to access do not necessarily tell us about the physical extent of the internet itself. Assuming you had the right device and the political freedom, is there anywhere left on Earth where the labyrinth of cable and wireless signals does not reach?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Answering this question begins with an explanation of the various tiers of internet access. The primary mechanisms for getting online are wired connections, mobile networks and satellites. Fibre-optic cables make up the core of the internet, &lt;a href=\"http://www.submarinecablemap.com/\"&gt;criss-crossing oceans&lt;/a&gt; and land. The first of those communications cables were put down in the 1850s for carrying telegraph signals. Today they &lt;a href=\"http://www.submarinecablemap.com/\"&gt;connect all continents&lt;/a&gt; except Antarctica, and include many &ndash; &lt;a href=\"http://www.nanog.org/meetings/nanog57/presentations/Tuesday/tues.general.Stronge.Submarine.32.pdf\"&gt;but not all&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; small island nations.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Mobile connections, meanwhile, rely on cell phone towers. And these can have an impressive reach. &ldquo;Two years ago I was in the Sahara, and for quite a large amount of time I had access,&rdquo; Graham-Cumming says. &ldquo;It was patchy and slow, but it was there.&rdquo; Indeed, many developing countries, especially in Africa, rely predominantly on mobile connections for accessing the internet.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Finally, satellites are the slowest means of getting online, but the only choice for those living far from a cell phone tower or wire. The &lt;a href=\"http://www.iridium.com/default.aspx\"&gt;Iridium&lt;/a&gt; satellite constellation coverage extends over the entire world, and their satellite phones can wire you up in otherwise unconnected places, such as national parks in the US, Antarctica or isolated spots of land like the Cook Islands. &ldquo;If you live out in the sticks somewhere, it makes no sense for your local telecom provider to run a fibre to your house or farm,&rdquo; says David Belson, editor of the quarterly &lt;a href=\"http://www.akamai.com/stateoftheinternet/\"&gt;State of the Internet&lt;/a&gt; report at &lt;a href=\"http://www.akamai.com/\"&gt;Akamai&lt;/a&gt;, one of the world&rsquo;s largest content delivery networks. &ldquo;So in many cases satellite is the optimal solution, although it may not be the fastest one.&rdquo; Sheer distance explains that delay: from the equator, for instance, data needs to travel about 22,000 miles (35,000km) between satellite and user.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01s8jbc\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Internet access via satellite is gradually improving, though. A satellite broadband provider called &lt;a href=\"http://www.o3bnetworks.com/\"&gt;O3b Networks&lt;/a&gt; recently launched its first four satellites, which it says orbit around &lt;a href=\"http://www.o3bnetworks.com/welcome-to-o3b/faqs\"&gt;four times closer to Earth&lt;/a&gt; than regular geosynchronous satellites and cover a 400 mile (643km) circumference per satellite. This should speed up data transfer by about four times compared to traditional satellite connections. The Cook Islands in the Pacific signed up as the first trial customers, while places like Somalia and inland Peru are expected to join in six months or so. The company also plans to provide internet to cruise ships and offshore oilrigs, which currently use traditional satellites. &ldquo;There will always be places where it&rsquo;s hard to get either terrestrial or satellite connections, but those pockets will become smaller and smaller,&rdquo; says Steve Collar, O3b&rsquo;s CEO.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Likewise, Google recently announced plans to tackle remaining internet deserts through its &lt;a href=\"http://www.google.com/loon/\"&gt;Loon&lt;/a&gt; project, a group of giant balloons that will fly at about 70,000 feet (21,000 metres) and deliver internet to rural or disaster-stricken areas. Like satellites, people connecting through the balloons would need a special antenna to deliver and receive a signal. The &lt;a href=\"http://www.google.com/loon/where/\"&gt;pilot launch&lt;/a&gt; took place last June, when 30 balloons were deployed above New Zealand&rsquo;s South Island.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01s8j38\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Other technologies also promise to make access easier in the small or temporary black spots still remaining in more developed nations. In underground tunnels, internet can be delivered by creating local hotspots using mobile radios, as is the case on Amtrak and Eurostar trains, and in some city subways.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Is coverage pretty much ubiquitous then? Not quite. There are a few places left where cable, wireless or satellite signals do not reach. Deep caves like Georgia&rsquo;s Krubera Cave, which reaches 5,610 feet (1,710m) underground, for one, would likely be devoid of service &ndash; it&rsquo;s the deepest cave on Earth. (However, even underground it wouldn&rsquo;t be guaranteed if there&rsquo;s a cell phone tower nearby, for example, or an opening directly overhead that a satellite signal could penetrate.)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another possible last place without internet is deep underwater. While many submarines have internet enabled through the same means that they use to keep up radio contact, the signals can be poor or non-existent because the signal can become distorted by water. &ldquo;I bet a nuclear submarine has really rubbish access,&rdquo; Graham-Cumming speculates.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In fact, the best way to get offline might be a self-imposed ban. It&rsquo;s possible that internet-free zones might emerge in the future. Some communities may choose to deliberately cut themselves off from the internet, much like the uncontacted tribes in South America, New Guinea and India, who purposefully choose to remain isolated. &ldquo;I wouldn&rsquo;t be surprised if some group eventually says, &lsquo;No, we don&rsquo;t want to have internet,&rsquo;&rdquo; says Graham-Cumming.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To truly avoid the internet, then, you have to make a great deal of effort. Even the remotest wilderness now yields a signal of some sort. So if you ever find yourself yearning for a life before emails, LOLcat memes and Facebook, consider this: the tendrils of the ultimate network are so widespread now, it&rsquo;s surprisingly difficult to escape.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-02-14T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"The last places on Earth without the internet","HeadlineShort":"The last places without internet","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Is there anywhere left on Earth where its impossible to access the internet? There are a few places, but you have to go out of your way to find them, discovers Rachel Nuwer.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Is there anywhere left on Earth where its impossible to access the internet? There are a few places, but it's not easy to find them, discovers Rachel Nuwer","SummaryShort":"Just how far does the network reach now?","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:48Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"abeda516-9571-4b0d-8781-eb6bcc1e29ff","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140214-the-last-places-without-internet","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:48Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140214-the-last-places-without-internet"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140214-the-last-places-without-internet","_id":"559a6a4082f2d2dbf7bd4d79"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;For the first 12 days, you go about your life normally, unaware that these may very well be your last.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Then at around day 13, the virus erupts out of infected cells and pours into your blood stream. Fever, headache, vomiting and intense body aches leave you too sick to function. At this point, however, there&rsquo;s still hope: perhaps you&rsquo;ve just contracted the flu.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Around day 17, however, those prayers are dashed. The first telltale spots begin to break out on your tongue and inside your mouth. These quickly spread across your body, concentrating in the extremities. The pea-sized bumps soon fill with a thick liquid &ndash; the detritus of your ravaged tissues &ndash; and develop a donut-like indentation. For those who dare touch them, they feel like small pebbles embedded under the skin. If you survive &ndash; and 30% of people do not &ndash; the papules will form a crust, eventually falling off as scabs and leaving you with life-long pitted scars.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That&rsquo;s what happens when you are infected by Variola major virus &ndash; better known as &lt;a href=\"http://www.bt.cdc.gov/agent/smallpox/overview/disease-facts.asp\"&gt;smallpox&lt;/a&gt;. An estimated 300 million people died from smallpox in the 20th Century alone, making it one of history&rsquo;s biggest ever viral killers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thankfully, however, smallpox was stamped out more than three decades ago, and now there are only a few samples of smallpox left on Earth. Where are they? Why are they there? And could 2014 be the year that we finally destroy the last of this killer virus once and for all?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 1975, the last naturally occurring case of Variola major turned up in a two-year old girl in Bangladesh. Finally, after three decades of eradication efforts, on 8 May&nbsp; 1980, the World Health Organization pronounced success, writing: &ldquo;The world and its peoples have won freedom from smallpox, which was a most devastating disease sweeping in epidemic form through many countries since earliest time, leaving death, blindness and disfigurement in its wake.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This was possible because unlike most deadly viruses &ndash; such as Ebola, yellow fever, HIV, or influenza &ndash; smallpox has no animal reservoir. Without human incubators, the virus cannot naturally exist.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It also doesn&rsquo;t stay alive on its own for long. In the past, some researchers and &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencemag.org/content/295/5562/2002\"&gt;news outlets&lt;/a&gt; speculated that smallpox in the frozen graves of former victims might remain in suspended animation, ready to begin a new cycle of infection should those bodies ever be dug up and thawed. Scientists have attempted to excavate corpses in frozen graves in Alaska and Siberia that contain the remains of smallpox victims, however none of the bodies contained viable viruses. &ldquo;No one feels there&rsquo;s a serious chance that global warming will melt the permafrost and unleash an epidemic,&rdquo; says Michael Lane, who served as director of the Centers for Disease Control&rsquo;s (CDC) smallpox eradication programme from 1970 until 1981, when it was declared a success and shut down.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Chilling case&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;After smallpox was eradicated from the environment, a handful of labs across the world kept live samples of the disease for research purposes, such as developing treatments and vaccines. In 1978, however, the dangers became clear. Janet Parker, a photographer working at the University of Birmingham Medical School, began complaining about headache and muscle pains. Days later, red spots appeared on her body, which medical workers wrote off as a harmless rash. Two weeks after her symptoms began, however, doctors correctly diagnosed smallpox. She was quarantined and treated for her illness, but died around two weeks later. Her mother also fell ill but survived, while her father suffered a fatal heart attack during a visit to see his daughter in the hospital.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Parker had somehow been exposed to the virus, which had likely traveled through air ducts to her office from a lab downstairs where researchers kept samples of smallpox. Henry Bedson, the head of the university&rsquo;s medical microbiology department, blamed himself for Parker&rsquo;s death and &lt;a href=\"http://news.google.com/newspapers?nid=2507&amp;dat=19780909&amp;id=PwI-AAAAIBAJ&amp;sjid=cUkMAAAAIBAJ&amp;pg=5226,1912288\"&gt;cut his throat&lt;/a&gt; several days before Parker passed away.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This distressing series of events triggered a complete rethink about keeping stores of the smallpox virus for research. &ldquo;Although we all had in the back of our minds that we needed to extirpate the virus &ndash; not just the disease &ndash; that incident made us realise that the repository of smallpox in test tubes and screw-top vials in various labs was a major problem,&rdquo; Lane says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The World Health Organization quietly put out a call for all samples of the virus to be either destroyed or sent to one of two official repositories: a CDC lab near Atlanta and the Vector Institute in Koltsovo, Siberia. A handful of samples quickly came in, arriving from India, Japan, the UK and other locations. &ldquo;It very quickly became obvious that nobody wanted the virus in their lab anyway,&rdquo; Lane says. &ldquo;They were delighted to give this stuff up.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Viral tomb&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; Currently, smallpox is known to be kept only at those two locations. (While previously unknown vials were &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/news/world-us-canada-28221185\"&gt;discovered in a Washington lab in July 2014&lt;/a&gt;, they were soon transported to Atlanta.) Both house the virus in biosafety level four (BSL-4) facilities. This level of safety &ndash; the highest possible &ndash; requires researchers to wear a positive pressure suit that resembles those worn in space and includes a tethered air supply. All manipulation with the virus, which is stored in liquid nitrogen tanks, is done in bio-safety cabinets. When exiting the lab, researchers spend seven minutes in a chemical shower to disinfect themselves, followed by a water rinse. No air escapes from within the BSL-4&rsquo;s rooms, and each year the CDC lab is shut down for a month to undergo preventative maintenance.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While the CDC is located on the fringes of bustling Atlanta, the Russian smallpox repository, Vector, has an additional safety measure &ndash; it is in the middle of nowhere.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Security at both locations is formidable. &ldquo;I was director of smallpox programmes for many years, and I live one mile from the CDC,&rdquo; Lane says. &ldquo;I can&rsquo;t even get into the building &ndash; not just the lab &ndash; where smallpox virus is kept.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Only a small number of researchers are permitted to enter these chambers. For some, it&rsquo;s just another day on the job. &ldquo;I cut my teeth working on BSL-4 viruses,&rdquo; says Peter Jahrling, director of the National Institute of Allergy and Infectious Disease&rsquo;s Integrated Research Facility, and chief of the Emerging Viral Pathogens Section. &ldquo;I work with Ebola, Lassa and Marburg, so smallpox is a walk in the park.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Others, however, maintain a sense of reverence for the disease. &ldquo;This is a virus that wreaked terrible havoc on the human population, and efforts to control and eradicate that disease were heroic,&rdquo; says Inger Damon, chief of the poxvirus and rabies branch at the CDC&rsquo;s Division of High-Consequence Pathogens and Pathology. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t think you ever become immune to that sense of awe.&rdquo;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Nowhere else?&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; So how do we know that additional smallpox samples are not also hoarded away in some terrorist hideout, or stuck in an old vial in the back of a forgotten freezer? &ldquo;We don&rsquo;t,&rdquo; Lane says. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s no way to prove a negative.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Still, things look good for the disease&rsquo;s confinement to the CDC and Vector. Since the late 1970s, as far as experts know, no one has contracted smallpox. At the same time, no terrorists have come forward claiming they have the virus and no rumours have circulated of it being kept in some secret lab.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Except, that is, for one suspicious case. In 1992, Ken Alibek &ndash; formerly Colonel Kanatzhan Alibekov &ndash; defected from Russia to the US. Alibek served as the first deputy director of Biopreparat, the Soviet Union&rsquo;s biological warfare agency, founded in 1973. After arriving in the US, Alibek began &lt;a href=\"http://www.nlm.nih.gov/nichsr/esmallpox/biohazard_alibek.pdf\"&gt;making allegations&lt;/a&gt; against his former nation, claiming that the Soviet Union had produced 50 tons of smallpox. Biopreparat, he claimed, had employed a scientific army of 30,000 people who worked on smallpox and other pathogenic weapons, such as Ebola, anthrax and plague.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Could this be true? &ldquo;It&rsquo;s no secret that smallpox virus was developed as a biological weapon and produced on an industrial scale,&rdquo; Jahrling says. &ldquo;Is it possible that someone kept a little retirement package in his freezer? It&rsquo;s not impossible.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But some &ndash; including Lane &ndash; doubt Alibek&rsquo;s claims, and point out there&rsquo;s no evidence. &ldquo;The fact that nobody knows for sure whether this happened is the major factor that makes people nervous, even though there&rsquo;s no solid reason to believe it,&rdquo; says Lane.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Last to go&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now the last remaining virus samples in Atlanta and Russia might finally be headed for eradication.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This summer, the World Health Assembly &ndash; which acts as a sort of congress for the WHO &ndash; will vote about whether or not to destroy the remaining smallpox samples. The Russian delegation has consistently voted against this path, so there is a chance that Russia would not comply if the vote turned in favour of destroying the virus. For the US, however, &ldquo;it would be extremely embarrassing for us not to go along with the official WHO recommendation,&rdquo; Lane says. &ldquo;We&rsquo;d basically be thumbing our nose at the UN.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Russians are not alone in wanting to keep smallpox. Some scientists say that more research needs to be done on the complex genetics of the virus, which could help devise more effective antivirals, for example. &ldquo;I know many in this country feel there&rsquo;s important research to be done, and that it really doesn&rsquo;t make a lot of sense to acquiesce to some symbolic act,&rdquo; Jahrling says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Damon also sees the logic in keeping smallpox around. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve changed my mind many times, and when I first came to this position at CDC, I didn&rsquo;t really see a need to use the live virus,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;But I think if we&rsquo;re going to develop a better understanding of the virus, having access to it is critical.&rdquo;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Dissenters, on the other hand, claim that most of that work can be done with the much less virulent Vaccinia virus or other related viruses. &ldquo;It almost becomes a theological argument: either you believe you need it or you don&rsquo;t,&rdquo; Jahrling says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Wouldn&rsquo;t destroying it make certain once and for all that it&rsquo;d never fall into the wrong hands though? The truth is, even it were destroyed, existing lab techniques could in principle recreate it based on the smallpox genome, which has been sequenced.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That begs the question: why hasn&rsquo;t anyone done that already? If a terrorist group, say, did have the means of creating smallpox, the effort may not be worth it compared with other methods of terrorism, such as the use of chemical weapons, says Lane. Vaccines exist to contain smallpox&rsquo;s spread, and it isn&rsquo;t the most contagious of pathogens. A smallpox outbreak would no doubt be a catastrophe, but there are worse things that could be unleashed on a population. We beat smallpox once and we could beat it again.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For now, at least, smallpox lives on &ndash; somewhere deep inside two very special labs; the only two places left on Earth where one of history&rsquo;s biggest killers still thrives.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-01-31T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Smallpox: Last refuge of an ultimate killer","HeadlineShort":"Last refuge of an ultimate killer","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"In July 2014, long forgotten vials of smallpox left in a cardboard box were discovered at a research centre near Washington. Until then, it was believed that only two places on Earth guard the last remaining samples of this killer virus. As Rachel Nuwer discovered in this feature, first published in January 2014, they may not be there for long.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"If a virus killed millions, why keep it alive? Two places on Earth guard the last vials of smallpox, but Rachel Nuwer finds that they may not be there for long","SummaryShort":"Why we havent fully eradicated smallpox","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:56Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"b80d587a-d796-4fb3-aca2-85c3bff360a1","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140130-last-refuge-of-an-ultimate-killer","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:56Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140130-last-refuge-of-an-ultimate-killer"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140130-last-refuge-of-an-ultimate-killer","_id":"559a6a3f82f2d2dbf7bd4d3f"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;A special kind of noisiness accosts passengers waiting for New York City subways. Down there, sound levels regularly exceed 100 decibels &ndash; enough to &lt;a href=\"http://www.newsday.com/news/new-york/subway-noise-can-damage-your-ears-say-experts-1.6164286\"&gt;damage a person&rsquo;s hearing over time&lt;/a&gt;. It was on one such platform that George Foy, a journalist and New York University creative writing professor, suddenly found himself losing it one day, when four trains pulled in at once. &ldquo;I kind of went momentarily crazy,&rdquo; he says. He hunched over and stuck his fingers in his ears, desperately trying to block out the cacophony. &ldquo;I started wondering why the hell I was putting up with this,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It was then that his obsession to find the quietest place on Earth began. &ldquo;I thought, &lsquo;If this is the craziness of noise, what is the opposite? What is absolute silence, and does it exist?&rsquo;&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Foy took it upon himself to seek out the world&rsquo;s quietest place, detailed in his recent book, &lt;a href=\"http://www.amazon.com/dp/1416599592\"&gt;Zero Decibels&lt;/a&gt;. He joins many others, ranging from &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120921-give-peace-and-quiet-a-chance\"&gt;health professionals to ecologists to hobbyists&lt;/a&gt;, who have attempted to seek out the quietest corners in the world. Yet it turns out that finding those unsullied locations is more difficult than it might seem. Foy, for one, did find his ultimate silent spot in the end, but it wasn&rsquo;t quite the peaceful refuge he anticipated &ndash; in fact, he discovered there that there&rsquo;s one human noise none of us can ever escape.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Sit still for a moment, and prick up your ears. What can you hear? Even in what seems like silence: you may soon notice the hum of your computer, the ticking of a clock, the electric burr of a refrigerator or an air conditioner, or the faint hum of a car passing by. Humanity&rsquo;s noises are always with us in one form or another.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So if you were seeking to escape this background buzz, where would you start? First let&rsquo;s assume that you&rsquo;d want somewhere reasonably pleasant to visit. So while deep underground might be an option, it wouldn&rsquo;t be very hospitable. Similarly, life underwater would be full of hardships &ndash; and in any case, you couldn&rsquo;t escape man-made sounds like scuba regulators or submarine engines because you&rsquo;d need them to stay alive. That&rsquo;s not to mention the underwater rumblings of ships, drilling and explosions that permeate the worlds&rsquo; oceans. In water, sound travels more than &lt;a href=\"http://oceanservice.noaa.gov/facts/sound.html\"&gt;four times faster than in air&lt;/a&gt;, and reaches further.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Silent checklist&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;On land, scratching noisy places off the list of potentials comes easily. Satellite images can be used to exclude all &lt;a href=\"http://eoimages.gsfc.nasa.gov/images/imagerecords/55000/55167/earth_lights.jpg\"&gt;areas with artificial light&lt;/a&gt;. Google Earth further reveals timber operations, mining, agriculture, roads and nautical shipping routes &ndash; all, presumably, rife with noise.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Following this logic, Antarctica immediately comes to mind as a promising candidate. It no doubt has silent corners &ndash; but the seasons do make things tricky. In the winter, you wouldn&rsquo;t want to go for a stroll. And in the summer, research planes and tourism boats are prevalent, and resident scientists power their camps with whirring diesel generators, the rumble of which can be heard for 20 miles or more around.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Other apparently remote regions of Earth might prove no better. Planes are the primary culprits. The rumblings of the largest aircraft can, on a clear day, travel up to 160km (100 miles). There is no altitude that a commercial jet can fly at which it is not audible on the ground, and the sound of any given aircraft can be heard, on average, between three to eight minutes as it passes overhead.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While no commercial flights travel across Antarctica, they do cruise over wildernesses such as northern Canada, Siberia or the Sahara. The North Pole is out too, as planes regularly traverse that route on trips between North America and Europe. Even deep in the Amazon rainforest, 1,900 km (1,200 miles) from the nearest city, Gordon Hempton, an acoustic ecologist and founder of the non&ndash;profit &lt;a href=\"http://onesquareinch.org/\"&gt;One Square Inch&lt;/a&gt;, recorded sounds from one or two aircraft per hour. &ldquo;Even if you are far from a road, you are not far from the roads in the sky,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Unfortunately, Hempton says that there is absolutely no place on Earth that is completely free from human sound all of the time. A map of the established flight paths over the US, for example, &ldquo;looks like a plate of spaghetti,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hempton is not alone in this pronouncement. &ldquo;There are no places on Earth that I&rsquo;ve been that haven&rsquo;t been affected by human sound,&rdquo; agrees Bernie Krause, an expert in bioacoustics and one of the founders of the field of &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soundscape_ecology\"&gt;soundscape ecology&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;All over the Earth, not a day goes by when you don&rsquo;t hear something.&rdquo; &lt;br /&gt; &lt;strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; Quiet winners&nbsp; &lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; Given the lack of truly silent places in nature, identifying the quietest place on Earth really boils down to how stringent you are about how long silence should last. Several years ago, the Campaign to Protect Rural England declared &lt;a href=\"http://www.visitalnwick.org.uk/explore/northumberland_national_park.htm\"&gt;a spot in Northumberland&lt;/a&gt; the most tranquil place in the country. One of the reasons that idyllic refuge is so quiet, however, is its location just next to a military training area. Away from all roads and accessible only by foot or mountain bike, that corner of the country is indeed very silent &ndash; except when tanks are rumbling across the base and soldiers are practicing on the firing range. &ldquo;If you go on a training day, it&rsquo;s anything but tranquil,&rdquo; says Trevor Cox, a professor of acoustic engineering at the University of Salford.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For the past 30 years, Hempton has made it his mission to discover what he calls the last great quiet places, areas that clock in at audible human noise-free intervals of 15 minutes or more. He only counts areas of around 3,100 square kilometres (1,200 square miles) or larger &ndash; enough to create a sound buffer around a central point of absolute quiet. Over the years, his list has shrunk as he returns to a previously quiet spot, only to find it now polluted by noise. Still, he says 12 such quiet places exist in the US, with more found around the world. A spot in the &lt;a href=\"http://www.nps.gov/olym/planyourvisit/visiting-the-hoh.htm\"&gt;Hoh Rainforest&lt;/a&gt; in Washington is one, as are places in &lt;a href=\"http://www.pc.gc.ca/pn-np/sk/grasslands/index.aspx\"&gt;Grasslands National Park&lt;/a&gt; in Canada, &lt;a href=\"http://www.bwca.com/\"&gt;Boundary Waters Canoe Area&lt;/a&gt; in Minnesota and &lt;a href=\"http://www.nps.gov/hale/index.htm\"&gt;Haleakala National Park&lt;/a&gt; in Hawaii (see gallery). The others, however, he keeps confidential.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although his search has focused on the US, he has investigated quiet places abroad, too. Germany, France, Spain, Switzerland, Austria and most developed parts of Europe and Asia have all been ruled out. On the other hand, he identified a quiet place &lt;a href=\"http://www.cofan.org/\"&gt;deep in the Ecuadorian rainforest&lt;/a&gt;, and there are others in South America. He suspects one might exist in Poland (he&rsquo;s waiting on funding to come through to further investigate this possibility), and also more in Norway, Sweden and Finland.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hempton and Krause hope that nations will adopt a quiet area program akin to &lt;a href=\"http://www.darksky.org/\"&gt;dark sky programs&lt;/a&gt;. They are pushing for the US National Park Service to adopt such regulations in 2016, in time for the agency&rsquo;s centennial. &ldquo;I absolutely believe we will have our quiet places,&rdquo; Hempton says. &ldquo;Just like we went through with water quality, things have to get really bad before we recognise them as a basic value and clean them up.&rdquo; &lt;strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; Yet for those seeking absolute relief from human noise for an indefinite period, the surest bet would finding one of the rooms specifically built for that purpose, called anechoic chambers. They are both sound proof and sound wave-absorbing. That was the conclusion reached by Foy, at least, after he visited such a chamber at Orfield Labs in Minnesota. It measures at &ndash;9.4 decibels, a figure less than zero decibels because any sound that does exist is below what the human ear can detect. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s supposed to be the quietest place on earth,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Where others tend to become uncomfortable in the disconcerting silence, Foy relished the chance to be completely cut off. But minutes into his stay in the chamber, he noticed that the silence was in fact broken. His own body, it turned out &ndash; his breathing, his heartbeat, even the scratchy sound his scalp made rubbing against his skull when he frowned &ndash; was betraying his quest for auditory nothingness. &ldquo;The only time you&rsquo;ll hear absolute silence is when you&rsquo;re in no position to hear it, because you&rsquo;re dead,&rdquo; he realized.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The quietest places in the world, then, are ones we have built, and yet, so long as we are living, we will never escape the noise of our own bodies.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;On the other hand, given the discomfort that many report experiencing in &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20130816-the-sound-of-deafening-silence\"&gt;absolute silence&lt;/a&gt;, it may instead be nature&rsquo;s melodies that our souls are really craving. And for now, it&rsquo;s still possible to find forests, seashores or mountains where you can relax for 15 minutes or more completely free from the clatter of human existence. If you find one of these serene sweet spots, however, it might be best to keep it quietly to yourself.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-01-17T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"The last place on Earth without human noise","HeadlineShort":"Earths last place without noise","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Is there anywhere left utterly free of man-made sound?                <h1 class="primary-heading" role="heading">
                How human culture influences our genetics
               </h1>
              </div>
             </div>
             <div class="secondary-header-wrapper">
              <div aria-hidden="true" class="bbccom_slot" data-advert-groups="4">
               <div class="bbccom_advert bbccom_display_none">
                <script type="text/javascript">
                 /*&lt;![CDATA[*/

          <!-- hero-unit end -->
          <div class="page-component-wrapper standard article-content" id="story-content">
           <!-- primary content start -->
           <div class="primary-content" role="main">
            <div class="mpu-wrapper">
             <div aria-hidden="true" class="bbccom_slot" data-advert-groups="1_2_3">
              <div class="bbccom_advert bbccom_display_none">
               <script type="text/javascript">
                /*&lt;![CDATA[*/

            <!-- story start -->
            <div class="primary-content-lining">
             <!-- top-unit start -->
             <div class="top-unit">
              <div class="introduction-wrapper">
               <p class="introduction">
                The way we eat, cook, explore and interact with others can influence our genes, says Jason G Goldman. So how will modern culture shape our children?
               </p>
              </div>
              <div id="share-tools-top">
               <ul class="share">
                <li class="share-item">
                 <div class="share-item-lining">
                  <a class="share-button facebook" data-share-destination="facebook" href="https://www.facebook.com/sharer/sharer.php?u=http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20140410-can-we-drive-our-own-evolution&amp;t=How%20human%20culture%20influences%20our%20genetics" target="_blank">
                   facebook
                   <span class="icon gelicon--facebook">
                   </span>
                  </a>
                 </div>
                </li>
                <li class="share-item">
                 <div class="share-item-lining">
                  <a class="share-button twitter" data-share-destination="twitter" href="https://twitter.com/intent/tweet?text=How%20human%20culture%20influences%20our%20genetics&amp;url=http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20140410-can-we-drive-our-own-evolution" target="_blank">
                   Twitter
                   <span class="icon gelicon--twitter">
                   </span>
                  </a>
                 </div>
                </li>
                <li class="share-item">
                 <div class="share-item-lining">
                  <a class="share-button google" data-share-destination="google" href="https://plus.google.com/share?url=http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20140410-can-we-drive-our-own-evolution" target="_blank">
                   Google +
                   <span class="icon gelicon--google-plus">
                   </span>
                  </a>
                 </div>
                </li>
                <li class="share-item">
                 <div class="share-item-lining">
                  <a class="share-button email" data-share-destination="email" href="mailto:?subject=Shared from BBC:How%20human%20culture%20influences%20our%20genetics&amp;body=http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20140410-can-we-drive-our-own-evolution" target="_blank">
                   Email
                   <span class="icon gelicon--mail">
                   </span>
                  </a>
                 </div>
                </li>
               </ul>
              </div>
             </div>
             <!-- top-unit end -->
             <!-- mid-unit start -->
             <div class="mid-unit">
             </div>
             <!-- mid-unit end -->
             <!-- bottom-unit start -->
             <div class="bottom-unit">
              <div class="byline">
               <span class="byline-heading">
               </span>
               <ul class="seperated-list source-attribution">
                <li class="seperated-list-item source-attribution-author">
                 <span class="index-body">
                  By Jason G Goldman
                 </span>
                </li>
               </ul>
               <span class="publication-date index-body">
                10 April 2014
               </span>
              </div>
              <div class="body-content">
               <p>
                You shouldn't be able to drink milk. Your ancestors couldn't. It is only in the last 9,000 years that human adults have gained that ability without becoming ill. Children could manage it, but it was only when we turned to dairy farming that adults acquired the ability to properly digest milk.
               </p>
               <p>
                It turns out that cultures with a history of dairy farming and milk drinking have a much higher frequency of lactose tolerance  and its associated gene  than those who don't.
               </p>
               <p>
                Drinking milk is just one of example of the way that traditions and cultural practices can influence the path of our evolution. Culture and genetics are traditionally thought of as two separate processes, but researchers are increasingly realising that they are intimately connected, each influencing the natural progression of the other. Scientists call it "gene-culture co-evolution." Why does it matter? If we can pin down how culture influences our genetic makeup  and how the same processes apply to other creatures too  then we can be better understand how the way we act as a society today could influence our future.
               </p>
               <p>
                Another example of how culture influences our genes is the relationship between yam farming and malaria resistance. Throughout much of Africa, people are in constant battle with malaria.
                <a href="http://www.cdc.gov/malaria/">
                 According to the CDC
                </a>
                , in 2010 there were some 219 million cases of malaria reported worldwide, and 660,000 were fatal. More than 90% of those who died lived in Africa.
               </p>
               <p>
               </p>
               <div class="inline-media inline-image">
                <a class="replace-image" data-additional-wrapper-class="" data-anchor-title="Mosquito (SPL)" data-caption="Malaria-carrying mosquitoes are a killer in Africa - but some people have a different disease that gives them protection (SPL)" data-caption-title="" data-is-portrait="false" data-is-watermarked="true" data-replace-image="true" data-replace-url="" href="http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/624_351/images/live/p0/1x/5c/p01x5ctz.jpg" title="Mosquito (SPL) (Credit: SPL)">
                 <div class="replace-image-additional-content">
                  <span class="icon-wrapper gel-icon-wrapper icon-wrapper-fullscreen">
                   <i aria-hidden="true" class="gelicon gelicon--fullscreen">
                   </i>
                  </span>
                  <div class="pointer">
                  </div>
                 </div>
                 View image of Mosquito (SPL) (Credit: SPL)
                </a>
               </div>
               <p>
                But there are some people who seem to have a natural defense force. Their red blood cells, normally shaped like flattened disks, are shaped instead like a crescent or sickle. Because of the odd shaped blood cells,
                <a href="http://www.nhlbi.nih.gov/health/health-topics/topics/sca/">
                 sickle-cell disease can lead to blockages in blood vessels
                </a>
                , which in turn cause pain and organ damage. Under normal circumstances, evolution keeps sickle-cell disease to a minimum because it can be so harmful and can reduce life expectancy. But because of a biological quirk, the sickle-cell gene can actually protect against malaria. So in parts of the world where malaria infection rates are extremely high, like Africa, natural selection may actually favour the sickle-shaped cells. In the gamble of life, protection against malaria may be preferable, even at the potential cost of suffering from sickle-cell disease.
               </p>
               <p>
                Here's what's interesting: those communities that farm yams have much higher rates of the sickle-cell gene than nearby communities with different agricultural practices. In order to cultivate yams, trees had to be chopped down. "The removal of trees had the effect of inadvertently increasing the amount of standing water when it rained, which provided better breeding grounds for malaria-carrying mosquitoes," writes biologist Kevin Laland of the University of St Andrews in
                <a href="http://www.nature.com/nrg/journal/v11/n2/abs/nrg2734.html">
                 Nature Reviews Genetics
                </a>
                . More mosquitos mean more malaria, creating the conditions for sickle-shaped cells to become adaptive.
               </p>
               <p>
                So while it's sickle-cell disease that's protective against malaria, it was a uniquely human behavior  yam farming  that allowed evolution to act.
               </p>
               <p>
                Not all examples of gene-culture co-evolution are quite as beneficial. Polynesians, for example, have a uniquely high prevalence of type II diabetes. It's among the highest worldwide, and is higher even than among neighbouring human populations. One group of researchers has discovered that the
                <a href="http://www.nature.com/ejhg/journal/v15/n5/abs/5201793a.html">
                 Polynesians have a particularly high frequency of a variant of a gene called
                 <em>
                  PPARGC1A
                 </em>
                </a>
                , and that may be responsible for their high frequency of type II diabetes, at least in part.
               </p>
               <p>
               </p>
               <div class="inline-media inline-image">
                <a class="replace-image" data-additional-wrapper-class="" data-anchor-title="(SPL)" data-caption="Did a historical culture of exploration make Polynesian people more prone to obesity today? (SPL)" data-caption-title="" data-is-portrait="false" data-is-watermarked="true" data-replace-image="true" data-replace-url="" href="http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/624_351/images/live/p0/1x/5d/p01x5d72.jpg" title="(SPL) (Credit: SPL)">
                 <div class="replace-image-additional-content">
                  <span class="icon-wrapper gel-icon-wrapper icon-wrapper-fullscreen">
                   <i aria-hidden="true" class="gelicon gelicon--fullscreen">
                   </i>
                  </span>
                  <div class="pointer">
                  </div>
                 </div>
                 View image of (SPL) (Credit: SPL)
                </a>
               </div>
               <p>
                Why are they so uniquely afflicted by this disease? The researchers think it may have something to do with their ancestors'
                <a href="http://ngm.nationalgeographic.com/2013/01/restless-genes/dobbs-text">
                 culture of exploration
                </a>
                . As the Polynesians settled the islands of the Pacific, they endured long voyages across the open ocean, and faced the stresses of cold and starvation. Those conditions may have encouraged "thrifty metabolism", which allows people to build up fat deposits more quickly when food is available. Natural selection may have increased the frequency of associated gene variants among them. However the sort of metabolism that would have been useful to explorers can lead to obesity and type II diabetes for individuals in modern cultures with consistent sources of nourishment. So modern Polynesians may have inherited a susceptibility to type II diabetes not because they lead a sedentary lifestyle, but because their ancestors decided to climb into some canoes and explore their planet.
               </p>
               <p>
                While these examples are perhaps the best understood examples of gene-culture co-evolution, researchers have identified scores of others. Our domestication of plants may have given a leg up to the genes that allow us to detoxify certain chemical compounds found in the plants we eat. Our history of exploring new territories and unfamiliar climates may have acted upon genes that allow us to tolerate more extreme heat or cold than our ancestors. The invention of cooking may have altered the evolution of our jaw muscles and our tooth enamel. The emergence of language and complex social cognition may have prompted natural selection to further guide the development of our brains and nervous systems.
               </p>
               <p>
               </p>
               <div class="inline-media inline-image">
                <a class="replace-image" data-additional-wrapper-class="" data-anchor-title="Dolphins (SPL)" data-caption="Dolphin mothers teach their young a specialised 'culture' of feeding, called sponging (SPL)" data-caption-title="" data-is-portrait="false" data-is-watermarked="true" data-replace-image="true" data-replace-url="" href="http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/624_351/images/live/p0/1x/5d/p01x5dd0.jpg" title="Dolphins (SPL) (Credit: SPL)">
                 <div class="replace-image-additional-content">
                  <span class="icon-wrapper gel-icon-wrapper icon-wrapper-fullscreen">
                   <i aria-hidden="true" class="gelicon gelicon--fullscreen">
                   </i>
                  </span>
                  <div class="pointer">
                  </div>
                 </div>
                 View image of Dolphins (SPL) (Credit: SPL)
                </a>
               </div>
               <p>
                It would be easy to assume that cultural influences are unique to humans. Yet some animal species have at least rudimentary cultures, and it would be silly to think that this couldn't influence their genetics just as ours does. It might be happening among the
                <a href="http://rspb.royalsocietypublishing.org/content/281/1782/20133245.short">
                 dolphins of Shark Bay, Australia
                </a>
                .
               </p>
               <p>
                A group of researchers led by University of New South Wales biologist Anna Kopps has been studying the bottlenose dolphins of the western part of the bay. A well known form of foraging among these dolphins is "sponging," a behavior that involves carrying a conical sponge to protect their faces as they root around the seafloor looking for food. It's not just an exciting example of tool use; it's also evidence of cultural transmission.
               </p>
               <p>
                The behavior, as Kopps points out, is "almost exclusively transmitted from mothers to their offspring through social learning". That means there's a tight correlation between it and the parts of the young dolphins' genomes that are passed on from their mothers.
               </p>
               <p>
                This link does not necessarily provide us with evidence that a cultural behavior has caused alterations in the genetic material, unlike the lactose tolerance, malaria resistance, and thrifty metabolism examples in humans. Still, it's a clue that there could be something more, a hint at a way in which cultural practices in dolphins might create an opportunity for natural selection to occur.
               </p>
               <p>
               </p>
               <div class="inline-media inline-image">
                <a class="replace-image" data-additional-wrapper-class="" data-anchor-title="Could wearing robot prosthetics influence our future evolution? (Thinkstock)" data-caption="Could wearing robot prosthetics influence our future evolution? (Thinkstock)" data-caption-title="" data-is-portrait="false" data-is-watermarked="true" data-replace-image="true" data-replace-url="" href="http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/624_351/images/live/p0/1x/5d/p01x5ddp.jpg" title="Could wearing robot prosthetics influence our future evolution? (Thinkstock) (Credit: Thinkstock)">
                 <div class="replace-image-additional-content">
                  <span class="icon-wrapper gel-icon-wrapper icon-wrapper-fullscreen">
                   <i aria-hidden="true" class="gelicon gelicon--fullscreen">
                   </i>
                  </span>
                  <div class="pointer">
                  </div>
                 </div>
                 View image of Could wearing robot prosthetics influence our future evolution? (Thinkstock) (Credit: Thinkstock)
                </a>
               </div>
               <p>
                Cultural influence on our own evolution continues apace, but it is currently nearly impossible to predict just how it will happen. What sorts of genetic adaptations will we see as a result of our technological culture? Will those adaptations apply universally, or only among some of us? How will human-machine interfaces, such as robotic prostheses or neural implants, affect our gene pools? Will the propensity for violent sports in some cultures lead to adaptations to protect against head trauma? And what are the questions we don't even yet realise we should be asking?
               </p>
               <p>
                It no longer makes sense to think of genetics and culture as two separate uninteracting monoliths. The difficulty is identifying how and if one is influencing the other. "This is the great challenge for the field of gene-culture co-evolution, and it is a formidable challenge," Laland writes. "Nonetheless, the best-researched examples, such as the lactose-tolerance case, not only show that gene-culture co-evolution occurs but also illustrate the means to establish this."
               </p>
               <p>
                <em>
                 If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our
                </em>
                <a href="https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture">
                 <em>
                  Facebook
                 </em>
                </a>
                <em>
                 or
                </em>
                <a href="https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts">
                 <em>
                  Google+
                 </em>
                </a>
                <em>
                 page, or message us on
                </em>
                <a href="https://twitter.com/BBC_Future">
                 <em>
                  Twitter
                 </em>
                </a>
                <em>
                 .
                </em>
               </p>
              </div>
             </div>
             <!-- bottom-unit end -->

  <script type="text/javascript">
   (function() {
    'use strict';

    var promoManager = {
        url: '',
        segments: ['a', 'b'],
        promoLoaded: false,
                makeUrl: function (variant, theme, win) {
            var loc = win? win.location : window.location,
                proto = loc.protocol,
                host = loc.host,
                url = proto + '//' + ((proto.match(/s:/i) &amp;&amp; !host.match(/^www\.(int|test)\./i))? 'ssl.' : 'www.'),
                themes = ['light', 'dark'];

            if ( host.match(/^(?:www|ssl)\.(int|test|stage|live)\.bbc\./i) ) {
                url += RegExp.$1 + '.';
            }
            else if ( host.match(/^pal\.sandbox\./i) ) {
                url += 'test.';
            }
 
                        theme = themes[ +(theme === themes[0]) ];
           
           return url + 'bbc.co.uk/navpromo/' + variant + '/' + theme;
        },
                validSegment: function (segment) {
            var validSegments = this.segments;
            
            for (var i = 0, len = validSegments.length; i &lt; len; i++) {
                if (validSegments[i] === segment) {
                    return segment;
                }
            }
            
            return validSegments[0];
        },
                init: function(node) {
            var disabledByCookie = (document.cookie.indexOf('ckns_orb_nopromo=1') &gt; -1),
                orbFullWidth     = (document.getElementById('orb-aside').offsetWidth &gt;= 1008),
                that = this;
            
            if (window.promomanagerOverride) {
                for (var p in promomanagerOverride) {
                    that[p] = promomanagerOverride[p];
                }
            }
                
            if ( window.orb.fig('uk') &amp;&amp; orbFullWidth &amp;&amp; !disabledByCookie ) {
                require(['orb/async/_footerpromo', 'istats-1'], function(promo, istats) {
                                        var mandolinEndDate = new Date().getTime() + (7 * 60 * 60 * 24) * 1000,
                        mandolin = new bbc.Mandolin('footer-promo', that.segments, {rate: 0.2, end: mandolinEndDate}),
                        segmentToRequest = that.validSegment(mandolin.getSegment());

                    that.url = (window.promomanagerOverride || that).makeUrl(segmentToRequest, 'light');

                    if (that.url) { 
                        promo.load(that.url, node, {
                                                          onSuccess: function(e) {
                                istats.addLabels({ 'campaignID': e.campaignID });
                                if (segmentToRequest === mandolin.getSegment()) {
                                    istats.addLabels({ 'promo_id_segment': e.campaignID + ':' + mandolin.getSegment() });
                                }
                                istats.track('internal', {region: node, linkLocation : 'orb-footer-promo'});

                                istats.log('display', 'orb-footer-promo-displayed', {campaignID : e.campaignID, testVariant: segmentToRequest});
                                node.className = node.className + ' orb-footer-promo-loaded';
                                promoManager.promoLoaded = true;
                                promoManager.event('promo-loaded').fire(e);
                             },
                             onError: function() {
                                istats.log('error', 'orb-footer-promo-failed');
                                document.cookie = 'ckns_orb_nopromo=1; expires=' + new Date(new Date().getTime() + 1000 * 60 * 10).toGMTString() + ';';
                             }
                        });   
                    }
                });
            }
        }
    };
    
        
    define('orb/promomanager', ['orb/lib/_event'], function (event) {
        event.mixin(promoManager);
        return promoManager;
    });
    
    require(['orb/promomanager'], function (promoManager) {
        promoManager.init(document.getElementById('orb-footer-promo'));
    })
})();
  </script>
